      <h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Etymology">Etymology</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Bahrain&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Etymology">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2> 
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:Bellin_-_Karte_von_der_K%C3%BCste_von_Arabien_c.1745_(crop).png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f0/Bellin_-_Karte_von_der_K%C3%BCste_von_Arabien_c.1745_%28crop%29.png/220px-Bellin_-_Karte_von_der_K%C3%BCste_von_Arabien_c.1745_%28crop%29.png" decoding="async" width="220" height="186" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f0/Bellin_-_Karte_von_der_K%C3%BCste_von_Arabien_c.1745_%28crop%29.png/330px-Bellin_-_Karte_von_der_K%C3%BCste_von_Arabien_c.1745_%28crop%29.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f0/Bellin_-_Karte_von_der_K%C3%BCste_von_Arabien_c.1745_%28crop%29.png/440px-Bellin_-_Karte_von_der_K%C3%BCste_von_Arabien_c.1745_%28crop%29.png 2x" data-file-width="789" data-file-height="666"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:Bellin_-_Karte_von_der_K%C3%BCste_von_Arabien_c.1745_(crop).png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div>A 1745 <a href="/wiki/Jacques-Nicolas_Bellin" title="Jacques-Nicolas Bellin">Bellin</a> map of the historical region of Bahrain
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p><i>Bahrayn</i> is the <a href="/wiki/Dual_form" class="mw-redirect" title="Dual form">dual form</a> of Arabic <i>bahr</i> ("sea"), so <i>al-Bahrayn</i> originally means "the two seas". However, the name has been lexicalised as a feminine <a href="/wiki/Proper_noun" class="mw-redirect" title="Proper noun">proper noun</a> and does not follow the grammatical rules for duals; thus its form is always <i>Bahrayn</i> and never <i>Bahrān</i>, the expected <a href="/wiki/Nominative" class="mw-redirect" title="Nominative">nominative</a> form. Endings are added to the word with no changes, as in the name of the national anthem <i>Bahraynunā</i> ("our Bahrain") or the <a href="/wiki/Demonym" title="Demonym">demonym</a> <i>Bahraynī</i>. The medieval grammarian <a href="/wiki/Ismail_ibn_Hammad_al-Jawhari" title="Ismail ibn Hammad al-Jawhari">al-Jawahari</a> commented on this saying that the more formally correct term <i>Bahrī</i> (lit. "belonging to the sea") would have been misunderstood and so was unused.<sup id="cite_ref-Faroughy_20-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Faroughy-20">[20]</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citing_sources" title="Wikipedia:Citing sources"><span title="This citation requires a reference to the specific page or range of pages in which the material appears. (November 2020)">page&nbsp;needed</span></a></i>]</sup> </p>
      <p>It remains disputed which "two seas" the name <i>Bahrayn</i> originally refers to.<sup id="cite_ref-EoI_21-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-EoI-21">[21]</a></sup> The term appears five times in the <a href="/wiki/Quran" title="Quran">Quran</a>, but does not refer to the modern island—originally known to the Arabs as <i>Awal</i>—but, rather, to all of Eastern Arabia (most notably <a href="/wiki/Al-Katif" class="mw-redirect" title="Al-Katif">al-Katif</a> and <a href="/wiki/Al-Ahsa_Oasis" title="Al-Ahsa Oasis">al-Hasa</a>).<sup id="cite_ref-EoI_21-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-EoI-21">[21]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>Today, Bahrain's "two seas" are generally taken to be the bay east and west of the island,<sup id="cite_ref-22" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-22">[22]</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citing_sources" title="Wikipedia:Citing sources"><span title="This citation requires a reference to the specific page or range of pages in which the material appears. (November 2020)">page&nbsp;needed</span></a></i>]</sup> the seas north and south of the island,<sup id="cite_ref-23" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-23">[23]</a></sup> or the salt and fresh water present above and below the ground.<sup id="cite_ref-Faroughy_20-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Faroughy-20">[20]</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citing_sources" title="Wikipedia:Citing sources"><span title="This citation requires a reference to the specific page or range of pages in which the material appears. (November 2020)">page&nbsp;needed</span></a></i>]</sup> In addition to wells, there are areas of the sea north of Bahrain where fresh water bubbles up in the middle of the saltwater as noted by visitors since antiquity.<sup id="cite_ref-24" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-24">[24]</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citing_sources" title="Wikipedia:Citing sources"><span title="This citation requires a reference to the specific page or range of pages in which the material appears. (November 2020)">page&nbsp;needed</span></a></i>]</sup> An alternate theory with regard to Bahrain's toponymy is offered by the al-Ahsa region, which suggests that the two seas were the <a href="/wiki/Persian_Gulf" title="Persian Gulf">Great Green Ocean</a> (the Persian Gulf) and a <a href="/wiki/Al-Asfar_Lake" title="Al-Asfar Lake">peaceful lake</a> on the Arabian mainland. </p>
      <p>Until the late <a href="/wiki/Middle_Ages" title="Middle Ages">Middle Ages</a>, "Bahrain" referred to the <a href="/wiki/Eastern_Arabia" title="Eastern Arabia">region of Eastern Arabia</a> that included Southern <a href="/wiki/Iraq" title="Iraq">Iraq</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kuwait" title="Kuwait">Kuwait</a>, <a href="/wiki/Al-Ahsa_Oasis" title="Al-Ahsa Oasis">Al-Hasa</a>, <a href="/wiki/Qatif" title="Qatif">Qatif</a>, and Bahrain. The region stretched from <a href="/wiki/Basra" title="Basra">Basra</a> in Iraq to the <a href="/wiki/Strait_of_Hormuz" title="Strait of Hormuz">Strait of Hormuz</a> in <a href="/wiki/Oman" title="Oman">Oman</a>. This was Iqlīm al-Bahrayn's "Bahrayn Province." The exact date at which the term "Bahrain" began to refer solely to the Awal archipelago is unknown.<sup id="cite_ref-Rentz_25-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Rentz-25">[25]</a></sup> The entire <a href="/wiki/Coastal_strip" class="mw-redirect" title="Coastal strip">coastal strip</a> of Eastern Arabia was known as "Bahrain" for a millennium.<sup id="cite_ref-maj_26-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-maj-26">[26]</a></sup> The island and kingdom were also commonly spelled <b>Bahrein</b><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTE''EB''1878_15-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTE''EB''1878-15">[15]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTE''EB''1911_27-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTE''EB''1911-27">[27]</a></sup> into the 1950s. </p> 
      <h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Bahrain&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: History">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       Main article: <a href="/wiki/History_of_Bahrain" title="History of Bahrain">History of Bahrain</a>
      </div> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Antiquity">Antiquity</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Bahrain&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Antiquity">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:AncientTombsOfBahrain.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/ff/AncientTombsOfBahrain.svg/220px-AncientTombsOfBahrain.svg.png" decoding="async" width="220" height="172" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/ff/AncientTombsOfBahrain.svg/330px-AncientTombsOfBahrain.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/ff/AncientTombsOfBahrain.svg/440px-AncientTombsOfBahrain.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="643" data-file-height="504"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:AncientTombsOfBahrain.svg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div>Map showing the locations of the <a href="/wiki/Dilmun_Burial_Mounds" title="Dilmun Burial Mounds">ancient burial mounds</a>. There are an estimated 350,000 burial mounds.
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:Persia_600ad.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/89/Persia_600ad.jpg/220px-Persia_600ad.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="125" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/89/Persia_600ad.jpg/330px-Persia_600ad.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/89/Persia_600ad.jpg/440px-Persia_600ad.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1098" data-file-height="623"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:Persia_600ad.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div>The <a href="/wiki/Sassanid_Empire" class="mw-redirect" title="Sassanid Empire">Persian Empire in Sassanid era</a> on the eve of the Arab conquest, c. 600 AD.
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p>Bahrain was home to <a href="/wiki/Dilmun" title="Dilmun">Dilmun</a>, an important <a href="/wiki/Bronze_Age" title="Bronze Age">Bronze Age</a> trade centre linking <a href="/wiki/Mesopotamia" title="Mesopotamia">Mesopotamia</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Indus_River" title="Indus River">Indus Valley</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-28" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-28">[28]</a></sup> Bahrain was later ruled by the <a href="/wiki/Assyrian_people" title="Assyrian people">Assyrians</a> and <a href="/wiki/Babylonia" title="Babylonia">Babylonians</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-29" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-29">[29]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>From the sixth to third century BC, Bahrain was part of the <a href="/wiki/Achaemenid_Empire" title="Achaemenid Empire">Achaemenid Empire</a>. By about 250 BC, <a href="/wiki/Parthia" title="Parthia">Parthia</a> brought the Persian Gulf under its control and extended its influence as far as Oman. The Parthians established garrisons along the southern coast of the Persian Gulf to control trade routes.<sup id="cite_ref-30" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-30">[30]</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citing_sources" title="Wikipedia:Citing sources"><span title="This citation requires a reference to the specific page or range of pages in which the material appears. (November 2020)">page&nbsp;needed</span></a></i>]</sup> </p>
      <p>During the <a href="/wiki/Classical_era" class="mw-redirect" title="Classical era">classical era</a>, Bahrain was referred to by the <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Greek" title="Ancient Greek">ancient Greeks</a> as <i><a href="/wiki/Tylos" title="Tylos">Tylos</a></i>, the centre of pearl trading, when the Greek admiral <a href="/wiki/Nearchus" title="Nearchus">Nearchus</a> serving under <a href="/wiki/Alexander_the_Great" title="Alexander the Great">Alexander the Great</a> landed on Bahrain.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELarsen198313_31-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELarsen198313-31">[31]</a></sup> Nearchus is believed to have been the first of Alexander's commanders to visit the island, and he found a verdant land that was part of a wide trading network; he recorded: "That on the island of Tylos, situated in the Persian Gulf, are large plantations of cotton trees, from which are manufactured clothes called <i>sindones</i>, of strongly differing degrees of value, some being costly, others less expensive. The use of these is not confined to India, but extends to Arabia."<sup id="cite_ref-32" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-32">[32]</a></sup> The Greek historian <a href="/wiki/Theophrastus" title="Theophrastus">Theophrastus</a> states that much of Bahrain was covered by these cotton trees and that Bahrain was famous for exporting walking canes engraved with emblems that were customarily carried in Babylon.<sup id="cite_ref-33" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-33">[33]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>Alexander had planned to settle Greek colonists in Bahrain, and although it is not clear that this happened on the scale he envisaged, Bahrain became very much part of the Hellenised world: the language of the upper classes was Greek (although <a href="/wiki/Aramaic_language" class="mw-redirect" title="Aramaic language">Aramaic</a> was in everyday use), while <a href="/wiki/Zeus" title="Zeus">Zeus</a> was worshipped in the form of the Arabian sun-god <a href="/wiki/Shams_(goddess)" class="mw-redirect" title="Shams (goddess)">Shams</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-34" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-34">[34]</a></sup> Bahrain even became the site of Greek athletic contests.<sup id="cite_ref-35" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-35">[35]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>The Greek historian <a href="/wiki/Strabo" title="Strabo">Strabo</a> believed the <a href="/wiki/Phoenicians" class="mw-redirect" title="Phoenicians">Phoenicians</a> originated from Bahrain.<sup id="cite_ref-36" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-36">[36]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Herodotus" title="Herodotus">Herodotus</a> also believed that the homeland of the Phoenicians was Bahrain.<sup id="cite_ref-37" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-37">[37]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-38" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-38">[38]</a></sup> This theory was accepted by the 19th-century German classicist <a href="/wiki/Arnold_Heeren" class="mw-redirect" title="Arnold Heeren">Arnold Heeren</a> who said that: "In the Greek geographers, for instance, we read of two islands, named Tyrus or <a href="/wiki/Tylos" title="Tylos">Tylos</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Arad,_Bahrain" title="Arad, Bahrain">Aradus</a>, which boasted that they were the mother country of the Phoenicians, and exhibited relics of Phoenician temples."<sup id="cite_ref-39" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-39">[39]</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citing_sources" title="Wikipedia:Citing sources"><span title="">title&nbsp;missing</span></a></i>]</sup> The people of <a href="/wiki/Tyre,_Lebanon" title="Tyre, Lebanon">Tyre</a>, in particular, have long maintained <a href="/wiki/Persian_Gulf" title="Persian Gulf">Persian Gulf</a> origins, and the similarity in the words "Tylos" and "Tyre" has been commented upon.<sup id="cite_ref-40" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-40">[40]</a></sup> However, there is little evidence of any human settlement at all on Bahrain during the time when such migration had supposedly taken place.<sup id="cite_ref-41" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-41">[41]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>The name Tylos is thought to be a Hellenisation of the Semitic <i>Tilmun</i> (from <a href="/wiki/Dilmun" title="Dilmun">Dilmun</a>).<sup id="cite_ref-42" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-42">[42]</a></sup> The term Tylos was commonly used for the islands until <a href="/wiki/Ptolemy" title="Ptolemy">Ptolemy</a>'s <i><a href="/wiki/Geographia_(Ptolemy)" class="mw-redirect" title="Geographia (Ptolemy)">Geographia</a></i> when the inhabitants are referred to as Thilouanoi.<sup id="cite_ref-Jean_Francois_Salles_p132_43-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Jean_Francois_Salles_p132-43">[43]</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citing_sources" title="Wikipedia:Citing sources"><span title="">title&nbsp;missing</span></a></i>]</sup> Some place names in Bahrain go back to the Tylos era; for instance the name of Arad, a residential suburb of <a href="/wiki/Muharraq" title="Muharraq">Muharraq</a>, is believed to originate from "Arados", the ancient Greek name for Muharraq.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELarsen198313_31-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELarsen198313-31">[31]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>In the 3rd century, <a href="/wiki/Ardashir_I" title="Ardashir I">Ardashir I</a>, the first ruler of the <a href="/wiki/Sassanid_dynasty" class="mw-redirect" title="Sassanid dynasty">Sassanid dynasty</a>, marched on Oman and Bahrain, where he defeated Sanatruq the ruler of Bahrain.<sup id="cite_ref-books.google_44-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-books.google-44">[44]</a></sup> At this time, Bahrain was known as <a href="/wiki/Mishmahig" class="mw-redirect" title="Mishmahig">Mishmahig</a> (which in <a href="/wiki/Pahlavi_language" class="mw-redirect" title="Pahlavi language">Middle-Persian/Pahlavi</a> means "ewe-fish").<sup id="cite_ref-45" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-45">[45]</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citing_sources" title="Wikipedia:Citing sources"><span title="">title&nbsp;missing</span></a></i>]</sup> </p>
      <p>Bahrain was also the site of worship of an ox deity called <a href="/wiki/Awal" title="Awal">Awal</a> (Arabic: <span lang="ar" dir="rtl">اوال</span>‎) Worshipers built a large statue to Awal in <a href="/wiki/Muharraq" title="Muharraq">Muharraq</a>, although it has now been lost. For many centuries after <i>Tylos</i>, Bahrain was known as <i>Awal</i>. By the 5th century, Bahrain became a centre for <a href="/wiki/Nestorianism" title="Nestorianism">Nestorian Christianity</a>, with the village <a href="/wiki/Samahij" class="mw-redirect" title="Samahij">Samahij</a><sup id="cite_ref-46" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-46">[46]</a></sup> as the seat of bishops. In 410, according to the <a href="/w/index.php?title=Oriental_Syriac_Church&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Oriental Syriac Church (page does not exist)">Oriental Syriac Church</a> synodal records, a bishop named Batai was excommunicated from the church in Bahrain.<sup id="cite_ref-Jean_Francois_Salles_p132_43-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Jean_Francois_Salles_p132-43">[43]</a></sup> As a sect, the Nestorians were often persecuted as heretics by the <a href="/wiki/Byzantine_Empire" title="Byzantine Empire">Byzantine Empire</a>, but Bahrain was outside the Empire's control, offering some safety. The names of several <a href="/wiki/Muharraq" title="Muharraq">Muharraq</a> villages today reflect Bahrain's Christian legacy, with <a href="/wiki/Al_Dair" title="Al Dair">Al Dair</a> meaning "the monastery". </p>
      <p>Bahrain's pre-Islamic population consisted of <a href="/wiki/Arab_Christians" title="Arab Christians">Christian Arabs</a> (mostly <a href="/wiki/Abd_al-Qays" class="mw-redirect" title="Abd al-Qays">Abd al-Qays</a>), Persians (<a href="/wiki/Zoroastrians" class="mw-redirect" title="Zoroastrians">Zoroastrians</a>), <a href="/wiki/History_of_the_Jews_in_Bahrain#Early_history" title="History of the Jews in Bahrain">Jews</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-orig_47-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-orig-47">[47]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Aramaic_language" class="mw-redirect" title="Aramaic language">Aramaic</a>-speaking agriculturalists.<sup id="cite_ref-ram_48-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ram-48">[48]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-om_49-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-om-49">[49]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-50" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-50">[50]</a></sup> According to <a href="/wiki/Robert_Bertram_Serjeant" title="Robert Bertram Serjeant">Robert Bertram Serjeant</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Baharna" class="mw-redirect" title="Baharna">Baharna</a> may be the <a href="/wiki/Arabization" title="Arabization">Arabised</a> "descendants of converts from the original population of Christians (Aramaeans), Jews and Persians inhabiting the island and cultivated coastal provinces of <a href="/wiki/Eastern_Arabia" title="Eastern Arabia">Eastern Arabia</a> at the time of the <a href="/wiki/Muslim_conquest" class="mw-redirect" title="Muslim conquest">Muslim conquest</a>".<sup id="cite_ref-ram_48-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ram-48">[48]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Robert_Bertram_Serjeant_1968_488_51-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Robert_Bertram_Serjeant_1968_488-51">[51]</a></sup> The sedentary people of pre-Islamic Bahrain were <a href="/wiki/Aramaic_language#Geographic_distribution" class="mw-redirect" title="Aramaic language">Aramaic speakers</a> and to some degree Persian speakers, while <a href="/wiki/Syriac_language" title="Syriac language">Syriac</a> functioned as a <a href="/wiki/Sacred_language" title="Sacred language">liturgical language</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-om_49-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-om-49">[49]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Time_of_Muhammad">Time of Muhammad</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Bahrain&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Time of Muhammad">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       Main article: <a href="/wiki/List_of_expeditions_of_Muhammad" title="List of expeditions of Muhammad">List of expeditions of Muhammad</a>
      </div> 
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:Muhammad_Bahrain_letter_facsimile.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f2/Muhammad_Bahrain_letter_facsimile.png/220px-Muhammad_Bahrain_letter_facsimile.png" decoding="async" width="220" height="132" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f2/Muhammad_Bahrain_letter_facsimile.png/330px-Muhammad_Bahrain_letter_facsimile.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f2/Muhammad_Bahrain_letter_facsimile.png/440px-Muhammad_Bahrain_letter_facsimile.png 2x" data-file-width="706" data-file-height="422"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:Muhammad_Bahrain_letter_facsimile.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div><a href="/wiki/Facsimile" title="Facsimile">Facsimile</a> of a letter sent by <a href="/wiki/Muhammad" title="Muhammad">Muhammad</a> to <a href="/wiki/Munzir_ibn-Sawa_al-Tamimi" class="mw-redirect" title="Munzir ibn-Sawa al-Tamimi">Munzir ibn-Sawa al-Tamimi</a>, governor of Bahrain in AD&nbsp;628
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p><a href="/wiki/Muhammad" title="Muhammad">Muhammad</a>'s first interaction with the people of Bahrain was the <a href="/wiki/Al_Kudr_Invasion" title="Al Kudr Invasion">Al Kudr Invasion</a>. Muhammad ordered a surprise attack on the <a href="/wiki/Banu_Salim" title="Banu Salim">Banu Salim</a> tribe for allegedly plotting to attack Medina. He had received news that some tribes were assembling an army in Bahrain and preparing to attack the mainland. But the tribesmen retreated when they learned Muhammad was leading an army to do battle with them.<sup id="cite_ref-52" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-52">[52]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-53" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-53">[53]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>Traditional Islamic accounts state that <a href="/wiki/Al-Ala%27a_Al-Hadrami" class="mw-redirect" title="Al-Ala'a Al-Hadrami">Al-Ala'a Al-Hadrami</a> was sent as an envoy during the <a href="/wiki/Expedition_of_Zayd_ibn_Harithah_(Hisma)" title="Expedition of Zayd ibn Harithah (Hisma)">Expedition of Zayd ibn Harithah (Hisma)</a><sup id="cite_ref-54" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-54">[54]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-history_55-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-history-55">[55]</a></sup> to the <a href="/wiki/Eastern_Arabia" title="Eastern Arabia">Bahrain region</a> by the prophet <a href="/wiki/Muhammad" title="Muhammad">Muhammad</a> in AD&nbsp;628 and that <a href="/wiki/Munzir_ibn_Sawa_Al_Tamimi" class="mw-redirect" title="Munzir ibn Sawa Al Tamimi">Munzir ibn Sawa Al Tamimi</a>, the local ruler, responded to his mission and converted the entire area.<sup id="cite_ref-56" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-56">[56]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-57" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-57">[57]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Middle_Ages">Middle Ages</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Bahrain&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Middle Ages">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
      <p>In 899, the <a href="/wiki/Qarmatians" title="Qarmatians">Qarmatians</a>, a <a href="/wiki/Millenarianism" title="Millenarianism">millenarian</a> <a href="/wiki/Isma%27ilism" title="Isma'ilism">Ismaili</a> Muslim sect, seized Bahrain, seeking to create a <a href="/wiki/Utopia" title="Utopia">utopian</a> society based on reason and redistribution of property among initiates. Thereafter, the Qarmatians demanded <a href="/wiki/Tribute" title="Tribute">tribute</a> from the caliph in <a href="/wiki/Baghdad" title="Baghdad">Baghdad</a>, and in 930 sacked <a href="/wiki/Mecca" title="Mecca">Mecca</a> and <a href="/wiki/Medina" title="Medina">Medina</a>, bringing the sacred <a href="/wiki/Black_Stone" title="Black Stone">Black Stone</a> back to their base in <a href="/wiki/Bahrain_(historical_region)" class="mw-redirect" title="Bahrain (historical region)">Ahsa</a>, in medieval Bahrain, for ransom. According to historian <a href="/wiki/Al-Juwayni" title="Al-Juwayni">Al-Juwayni</a>, the stone was returned 22 years later in 951 under mysterious circumstances. Wrapped in a sack, it was thrown into the <a href="/wiki/Great_Mosque_of_Kufa" title="Great Mosque of Kufa">Great Mosque of Kufa</a> in Iraq, accompanied by a note saying "By command we took it, and by command we have brought it back." The theft and removal of the Black Stone caused it to break into seven pieces.<sup id="cite_ref-58" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-58">[58]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-glasse_59-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-glasse-59">[59]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-60" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-60">[60]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>Following their 976 defeat by the <a href="/wiki/Abbasid_Caliphate" title="Abbasid Caliphate">Abbasids</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-autogenerated2_61-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated2-61">[61]</a></sup> the Qarmatians were overthrown by the Arab <a href="/wiki/Uyunid_dynasty" title="Uyunid dynasty">Uyunid dynasty</a> of <a href="/wiki/Al-Ahsa_Oasis" title="Al-Ahsa Oasis">al-Hasa</a>, who took over the entire Bahrain region in 1076.<sup id="cite_ref-62" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-62">[62]</a></sup> The Uyunids controlled Bahrain until 1235, when the archipelago was briefly occupied by the Persian ruler of <a href="/wiki/Fars_Province" title="Fars Province">Fars</a>. In 1253, the <a href="/wiki/Bedouin" title="Bedouin">Bedouin</a> <a href="/wiki/Usfurids" title="Usfurids">Usfurids</a> brought down the Uyunid dynasty, thereby gaining control over eastern <a href="/wiki/Arabia" class="mw-redirect" title="Arabia">Arabia</a>, including the islands of Bahrain. In 1330, the archipelago became a tributary state of the rulers of <a href="/wiki/Ormus" title="Ormus">Hormuz</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-Rentz_25-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Rentz-25">[25]</a></sup> though locally the islands were controlled by the Shi'ite <a href="/wiki/Jarwanids" class="mw-redirect" title="Jarwanids">Jarwanid</a> dynasty of <a href="/wiki/Qatif" title="Qatif">Qatif</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-63" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-63">[63]</a></sup> In the mid-15th century, the archipelago came under the rule of the <a href="/wiki/Jabrids" title="Jabrids">Jabrids</a>, a Bedouin dynasty also based in <a href="/wiki/Al-Ahsa_Oasis" title="Al-Ahsa Oasis">Al-Ahsa</a> that ruled most of eastern Arabia. </p> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Early_modern_era">Early modern era</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Bahrain&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Early modern era">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Bani_Utbah_invasion_of_Bahrain" title="Bani Utbah invasion of Bahrain">Bani Utbah invasion of Bahrain</a>, <a href="/wiki/History_of_Bahrain_(1783%E2%80%931971)" title="History of Bahrain (1783–1971)">History of Bahrain (1783–1971)</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Qatari%E2%80%93Bahraini_War" title="Qatari–Bahraini War">Qatari–Bahraini War</a>
      </div> 
      <div class="thumb tleft">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:Bahrain_Fort_overview.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8f/Bahrain_Fort_overview.jpg/220px-Bahrain_Fort_overview.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="148" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8f/Bahrain_Fort_overview.jpg/330px-Bahrain_Fort_overview.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8f/Bahrain_Fort_overview.jpg/440px-Bahrain_Fort_overview.jpg 2x" data-file-width="734" data-file-height="493"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:Bahrain_Fort_overview.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div>The <a href="/wiki/Qal%27at_al-Bahrain" title="Qal'at al-Bahrain">Portuguese Fort of Barém</a>, built by the <a href="/wiki/Portuguese_Empire" title="Portuguese Empire">Portuguese Empire</a> while it ruled Bahrain from 1521 to 1602.
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:AradFort.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/13/AradFort.jpg/220px-AradFort.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/13/AradFort.jpg/330px-AradFort.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/13/AradFort.jpg/440px-AradFort.jpg 2x" data-file-width="640" data-file-height="480"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:AradFort.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div><a href="/wiki/Arad_Fort" title="Arad Fort">Arad Fort</a> in <a href="/wiki/Arad,_Bahrain" title="Arad, Bahrain">Arad</a>; constructed before the Portuguese assumed control.
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p>In 1521, the <a href="/wiki/Portuguese_Empire" title="Portuguese Empire">Portuguese Empire</a> allied with Hormuz and seized Bahrain from the <a href="/wiki/Jabrids" title="Jabrids">Jabrid</a> ruler <a href="/wiki/Muqrin_ibn_Zamil" title="Muqrin ibn Zamil">Muqrin ibn Zamil</a>, who was killed during the takeover. Portuguese rule lasted for around 80 years, during which time they depended mainly on <a href="/wiki/Sunni_Islam" title="Sunni Islam">Sunni</a> Persian governors.<sup id="cite_ref-Rentz_25-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Rentz-25">[25]</a></sup> The Portuguese were expelled from the islands in 1602 by <a href="/wiki/Abbas_I_of_Persia" class="mw-redirect" title="Abbas I of Persia">Abbas I</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Safavid_Empire" class="mw-redirect" title="Safavid Empire">Safavid Empire</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-64" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-64">[64]</a></sup> which gave impetus to <a href="/wiki/Shia_Islam" title="Shia Islam">Shia Islam</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-65" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-65">[65]</a></sup> For the next two centuries, Persian rulers retained control of the archipelago, interrupted by the 1717 and 1738 invasions of the <a href="/wiki/Ibadi" class="mw-redirect" title="Ibadi">Ibadis</a> of Oman.<sup id="cite_ref-autogenerated4_66-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated4-66">[66]</a></sup> During most of this period, they resorted to governing Bahrain indirectly, either through the city of <a href="/wiki/Bushehr" title="Bushehr">Bushehr</a> or through <a href="/wiki/Immigration" title="Immigration">immigrant</a> Sunni Arab clans. The latter were tribes returning to the Arabian side of the Persian Gulf from Persian territories in the north who were known as <i><a href="/wiki/Huwala" class="mw-redirect" title="Huwala">Huwala</a></i>.<sup id="cite_ref-Rentz_25-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Rentz-25">[25]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-67" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-67">[67]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-McCoy_68-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-McCoy-68">[68]</a></sup> In 1753, the Huwala clan of <a href="/wiki/Nasr_Al-Madhkur" title="Nasr Al-Madhkur">Nasr Al-Madhkur</a> invaded Bahrain on behalf of the Iranian <a href="/wiki/Zand_dynasty" title="Zand dynasty">Zand</a> leader <a href="/wiki/Karim_Khan_Zand" title="Karim Khan Zand">Karim Khan Zand</a> and restored direct Iranian rule.<sup id="cite_ref-McCoy_68-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-McCoy-68">[68]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>In 1783, Al-Madhkur lost the islands of Bahrain following his defeat by the <a href="/wiki/Bani_Utbah" title="Bani Utbah">Bani Utbah</a> tribe at the 1782 Battle of <a href="/wiki/Zubarah" title="Zubarah">Zubarah</a>. Bahrain was not new territory to the Bani Utbah; they had been a presence there since the 17th century.<sup id="cite_ref-69" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-69">[69]</a></sup> During that time, they started purchasing date palm gardens in Bahrain; a document shows that 81 years before arrival of the Al-Khalifa, one of the sheikhs of the <a href="/wiki/Al_Bin_Ali" title="Al Bin Ali">Al Bin Ali</a> tribe (an offshoot of the Bani Utbah) had bought a palm garden from Mariam bint Ahmed Al Sanadi in <a href="/wiki/Sitra" title="Sitra">Sitra</a> island.<sup id="cite_ref-70" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-70">[70]</a></sup> </p> 
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:Persian_Gulf_Pt8.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d6/Persian_Gulf_Pt8.png/220px-Persian_Gulf_Pt8.png" decoding="async" width="220" height="121" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d6/Persian_Gulf_Pt8.png/330px-Persian_Gulf_Pt8.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d6/Persian_Gulf_Pt8.png/440px-Persian_Gulf_Pt8.png 2x" data-file-width="900" data-file-height="494"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:Persian_Gulf_Pt8.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div>Purple - <a href="/wiki/Portuguese_empire" class="mw-redirect" title="Portuguese empire">Portuguese</a> in Persian Gulf in the 16th and 17th century. Main cities, ports and routes.
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p>The Al Bin Ali were the dominant group controlling the town of Zubarah on the Qatar peninsula,<sup id="cite_ref-Arabia's_Frontiers_p44_71-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Arabia's_Frontiers_p44-71">[71]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-72" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-72">[72]</a></sup> originally the center of power of the Bani Utbah. After the Bani Utbah gained control of Bahrain, the Al Bin Ali had a practically independent status there as a self-governing tribe. They used a flag with four red and three white stripes, called the <a href="/wiki/Al-Sulami_flag" title="Al-Sulami flag">Al-Sulami flag</a><sup id="cite_ref-73" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-73">[73]</a></sup> in Bahrain, Qatar, <a href="/wiki/Kuwait" title="Kuwait">Kuwait</a>, and the Eastern province of the Kingdom of Saudi Arabia. Later, different Arab family clans and tribes from Qatar moved to Bahrain to settle after the fall of Nasr Al-Madhkur of <a href="/wiki/Bushehr" title="Bushehr">Bushehr</a>. These families included the <a href="/wiki/House_of_Khalifa" title="House of Khalifa">House of Khalifa</a>, Al-Ma'awdah, Al-Fadhil, Al-Mannai, Al-Noaimi, Al-Sulaiti, Al-Sadah, Al-Thawadi and other families and tribes.<sup id="cite_ref-74" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-74">[74]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>The House of Khalifa moved from Qatar to Bahrain in 1799. Originally, their ancestors were expelled from <a href="/wiki/Umm_Qasr" title="Umm Qasr">Umm Qasr</a> in central Arabia by the <a href="/wiki/Ottoman_Empire" title="Ottoman Empire">Ottomans</a> due to their predatory habits of preying on caravans in <a href="/wiki/Basra" title="Basra">Basra</a> and trading ships in <a href="/wiki/Shatt_al-Arab" title="Shatt al-Arab">Shatt al-Arab</a> waterway until Turks expelled them to Kuwait in 1716, where they remained until 1766.<sup id="cite_ref-qdl1_75-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-qdl1-75">[75]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>Around the 1760s, the <a href="/wiki/Al_Jalahma" title="Al Jalahma">Al Jalahma</a> and House of Khalifa, both belonging to the Utub Federation, migrated to <a href="/wiki/Zubarah" title="Zubarah">Zubarah</a> in modern-day <a href="/wiki/Qatar" title="Qatar">Qatar</a>, leaving Al Sabah as the sole proprietors of Kuwait.<sup id="cite_ref-76" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-76">[76]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="19th_century_and_later">19th century and later</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Bahrain&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: 19th century and later">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
      <p>In the early 19th century, Bahrain was invaded by both the Omanis and the <a href="/wiki/House_of_Saud" title="House of Saud">Al Sauds</a>. In 1802 it was governed by a 12-year-old child, when the Omani ruler Sayyid Sultan installed his son, Salim, as governor in the <a href="/wiki/Arad_Fort" title="Arad Fort">Arad Fort</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-77" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-77">[77]</a></sup> In 1816, the British political resident in the Persian Gulf, William Bruce, received a letter from the Sheikh of Bahrain who was concerned about a rumour that Britain would support an attack on the island by the Imam of Muscat. He sailed to Bahrain to reassure the Sheikh that this was not the case and drew up an informal agreement assuring the Sheikh that Britain would remain a neutral party.<sup id="cite_ref-78" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-78">[78]</a></sup> </p> 
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:Coronation_of_Hamad_bin_Isa_Al_Khalifa_1933.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1d/Coronation_of_Hamad_bin_Isa_Al_Khalifa_1933.jpg/220px-Coronation_of_Hamad_bin_Isa_Al_Khalifa_1933.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="132" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1d/Coronation_of_Hamad_bin_Isa_Al_Khalifa_1933.jpg/330px-Coronation_of_Hamad_bin_Isa_Al_Khalifa_1933.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1d/Coronation_of_Hamad_bin_Isa_Al_Khalifa_1933.jpg/440px-Coronation_of_Hamad_bin_Isa_Al_Khalifa_1933.jpg 2x" data-file-width="800" data-file-height="479"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:Coronation_of_Hamad_bin_Isa_Al_Khalifa_1933.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div>This photograph shows the coronation of Hamad bin Isa Al Khalifa as the Hakim of Bahrain in February 1933.
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p>In 1820, the Al Khalifa tribe were recognised by the United Kingdom as the rulers ("Al-Hakim" in Arabic) of Bahrain after signing a <a href="/wiki/General_Maritime_Treaty_of_1820" title="General Maritime Treaty of 1820">treaty relationship</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-79" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-79">[79]</a></sup> However, ten years later they were forced to pay yearly tributes to Egypt despite seeking Persian and British protection.<sup id="cite_ref-NAS_80-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NAS-80">[80]</a></sup> </p> 
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:Trigonometrical_Plan_of_the_Island_and_Harbour_of_Bahrain.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7a/Trigonometrical_Plan_of_the_Island_and_Harbour_of_Bahrain.jpg/300px-Trigonometrical_Plan_of_the_Island_and_Harbour_of_Bahrain.jpg" decoding="async" width="300" height="340" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7a/Trigonometrical_Plan_of_the_Island_and_Harbour_of_Bahrain.jpg/450px-Trigonometrical_Plan_of_the_Island_and_Harbour_of_Bahrain.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7a/Trigonometrical_Plan_of_the_Island_and_Harbour_of_Bahrain.jpg/600px-Trigonometrical_Plan_of_the_Island_and_Harbour_of_Bahrain.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2098" data-file-height="2376"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:Trigonometrical_Plan_of_the_Island_and_Harbour_of_Bahrain.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div>Map of Bahrain in 1825.
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p>In 1860, the Al Khalifas used the same tactic when the British tried to overpower Bahrain. Writing letters to the Persians and <a href="/wiki/Ottoman_Empire" title="Ottoman Empire">Ottomans</a>, Al Khalifas agreed to place Bahrain under the latter's protection in March due to offering better conditions. Eventually the <a href="/wiki/British_Raj" title="British Raj">Government of British India</a> overpowered Bahrain when the Persians refused to protect it. Colonel <a href="/wiki/Lewis_Pelly" title="Lewis Pelly">Pelly</a> signed a new treaty with Al Khalifas placing Bahrain under British rule and protection.<sup id="cite_ref-NAS_80-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NAS-80">[80]</a></sup> </p> 
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:Bahrainharbor.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fa/Bahrainharbor.jpg/300px-Bahrainharbor.jpg" decoding="async" width="300" height="79" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fa/Bahrainharbor.jpg/450px-Bahrainharbor.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fa/Bahrainharbor.jpg/600px-Bahrainharbor.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1106" data-file-height="290"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:Bahrainharbor.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div>Manama harbor, c. 1870
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p>Following the <a href="/wiki/Qatari%E2%80%93Bahraini_War" title="Qatari–Bahraini War">Qatari–Bahraini War</a> in 1868, British representatives signed another agreement with the Al Khalifas. It specified that the ruler could not dispose of any of his territory except to the United Kingdom and could not enter into relationships with any foreign government without British consent.<sup id="cite_ref-81" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-81">[81]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-TPG_82-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-TPG-82">[82]</a></sup> In return the British promised to protect Bahrain from all aggression by sea and to lend support in case of land attack.<sup id="cite_ref-TPG_82-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-TPG-82">[82]</a></sup> More importantly the British promised to support the rule of the Al Khalifa in Bahrain, securing its unstable position as rulers of the country. Other agreements in 1880 and 1892 sealed the protectorate status of Bahrain to the British.<sup id="cite_ref-TPG_82-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-TPG-82">[82]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>Unrest amongst the people of Bahrain began when Britain officially established complete dominance over the territory in 1892. The first revolt and widespread uprising took place in March 1895 against Sheikh Issa bin Ali, then ruler of Bahrain.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMojtahed-Zadeh1999130_83-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMojtahed-Zadeh1999130-83">[83]</a></sup> Sheikh Issa was the first of the Al Khalifa to rule without Persian relations. <a href="/wiki/Arnold_Wilson" title="Arnold Wilson">Sir Arnold Wilson</a>, Britain's representative in the Persian Gulf and author of <i>The Persian Gulf</i>, arrived in Bahrain from Muscat at this time.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMojtahed-Zadeh1999130_83-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMojtahed-Zadeh1999130-83">[83]</a></sup> The uprising developed further with some protesters killed by British forces.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMojtahed-Zadeh1999130_83-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMojtahed-Zadeh1999130-83">[83]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>Before the development of petroleum, the island was largely devoted to <a href="/wiki/Pearl_hunting" title="Pearl hunting">pearl fisheries</a> and, as late as the 19th century, was considered to be the finest in the world.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTE''EB''1878_15-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTE''EB''1878-15">[15]</a></sup> In 1903, German explorer, <a href="/wiki/Hermann_Burchardt" title="Hermann Burchardt">Hermann Burchardt</a>, visited Bahrain and took many photographs of historical sites, including the old <i>Qaṣr es-Sheikh</i>, photos now stored at the <a href="/wiki/Ethnological_Museum_of_Berlin" title="Ethnological Museum of Berlin">Ethnological Museum of Berlin</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-84" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-84">[84]</a></sup> Prior to the <a href="/wiki/World_War_I" title="World War I">First World War</a>, there were about 400 vessels hunting pearls and an annual export of more than £30,000.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTE''EB''1911_27-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTE''EB''1911-27">[27]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>In 1911, a group of Bahraini merchants demanded restrictions on the British influence in the country. The group's leaders were subsequently arrested and exiled to India. In 1923, the British introduced <a href="/wiki/Bahrain_administrative_reforms_of_the_1920s" title="Bahrain administrative reforms of the 1920s">administrative reforms</a> and replaced Sheikh Issa bin Ali with his son. Some clerical opponents and families such as <a href="/wiki/Dawasir" title="Dawasir">al Dossari</a> left or were exiled to Saudi Arabia and Iran.<sup id="cite_ref-85" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-85">[85]</a></sup> Three years later the British placed the country under the <i>de facto</i> rule of <a href="/wiki/Charles_Belgrave" title="Charles Belgrave">Charles Belgrave</a> who operated as an adviser to the ruler until 1957.<sup id="cite_ref-All_at_sea_over_'the_Gulf'_86-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-All_at_sea_over_'the_Gulf'-86">[86]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-archiveeditions_87-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-archiveeditions-87">[87]</a></sup> Belgrave brought a number of reforms such as establishment of the country's first modern school in 1919, the Persian Gulf's first girls' school in 1928<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (June 2019)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> and the abolition of <a href="/wiki/Arab_slave_trade" title="Arab slave trade">slavery</a> in 1937.<sup id="cite_ref-88" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-88">[88]</a></sup> At the same time, the pearl diving industry developed at a rapid pace. </p>
      <p>In 1927, <a href="/wiki/Rez%C4%81_Sh%C4%81h" class="mw-redirect" title="Rezā Shāh">Rezā Shāh</a>, then <a href="/wiki/Shah_of_Iran" class="mw-redirect" title="Shah of Iran">Shah of Iran</a>, demanded sovereignty over Bahrain in a letter to the <a href="/wiki/League_of_Nations" title="League of Nations">League of Nations</a>, a move that prompted Belgrave to undertake harsh measures including encouraging conflicts between <a href="/wiki/Shia_Islam" title="Shia Islam">Shia</a> and Sunni Muslims in order to bring down the uprisings and limit the Iranian influence.<sup id="cite_ref-ICB_89-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ICB-89">[89]</a></sup> Belgrave even went further by suggesting to rename the <a href="/wiki/Persian_Gulf" title="Persian Gulf">Persian Gulf</a> to the "Arabian Gulf"; however, the proposal was refused by the British government.<sup id="cite_ref-All_at_sea_over_'the_Gulf'_86-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-All_at_sea_over_'the_Gulf'-86">[86]</a></sup> Britain's interest in Bahrain's development was motivated by concerns over Saudi and Iranian ambitions in the region. </p> 
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:First_Oil_Well,_Bahrain.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/41/First_Oil_Well%2C_Bahrain.jpg/220px-First_Oil_Well%2C_Bahrain.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="146" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/41/First_Oil_Well%2C_Bahrain.jpg/330px-First_Oil_Well%2C_Bahrain.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/41/First_Oil_Well%2C_Bahrain.jpg/440px-First_Oil_Well%2C_Bahrain.jpg 2x" data-file-width="530" data-file-height="351"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:First_Oil_Well,_Bahrain.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div>A photograph of the First Oil Well in Bahrain, with oil first being extracted in 1931
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p>The <a href="/wiki/Bahrain_Petroleum_Company" title="Bahrain Petroleum Company">Bahrain Petroleum Company</a> (Bapco), a subsidiary of the <a href="/wiki/Standard_Oil_Company" class="mw-redirect" title="Standard Oil Company">Standard Oil Company</a> of California (Socal),<sup id="cite_ref-90" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-90">[90]</a></sup> discovered oil in 1932.<sup id="cite_ref-91" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-91">[91]</a></sup> This was to bring rapid modernisation to Bahrain. Relations with the United Kingdom became closer, as evidenced by the British <a href="/wiki/Royal_Navy" title="Royal Navy">Royal Navy</a> moving its entire Middle Eastern command from <a href="/wiki/Bushehr" title="Bushehr">Bushehr</a> in <a href="/wiki/Iran" title="Iran">Iran</a> to Bahrain in 1935.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (August 2019)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> </p>
      <p>In the early 1930s, Bahrain Airport was developed. <a href="/wiki/Imperial_Airways" title="Imperial Airways">Imperial Airways</a> flew there, including the <a href="/wiki/Handley_Page_HP42" class="mw-redirect" title="Handley Page HP42">Handley Page HP42</a> aircraft. Later in the same decade the Bahrain Maritime Airport was established, for flying-boats and seaplanes.<sup id="cite_ref-92" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-92">[92]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>Bahrain <a href="/wiki/Participants_in_World_War_II#Bahrain" class="mw-redirect" title="Participants in World War II">participated</a> in the <a href="/wiki/World_War_II" title="World War II">Second World War</a> on the <a href="/wiki/Allies_of_World_War_II" title="Allies of World War II">Allied</a> side, joining on 10 September 1939. On 19 October 1940, four Italian <a href="/wiki/Savoia-Marchetti_SM.82" title="Savoia-Marchetti SM.82">SM.82s</a> bombers <a href="/wiki/Bombing_of_Bahrain_in_World_War_II" title="Bombing of Bahrain in World War II">bombed Bahrain</a> alongside <a href="/wiki/Dhahran" title="Dhahran">Dhahran</a> oilfields in Saudi Arabia,<sup id="cite_ref-93" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-93">[93]</a></sup> targeting Allied-operated oil refineries.<sup id="cite_ref-Hamza_94-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hamza-94">[94]</a></sup> Although minimal damage was caused in both locations, the attack forced the Allies to upgrade Bahrain's defences, an action which further stretched Allied military resources.<sup id="cite_ref-Hamza_94-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hamza-94">[94]</a></sup> </p> 
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:View_of_Manama_from_the_South,_1953.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6a/View_of_Manama_from_the_South%2C_1953.png/220px-View_of_Manama_from_the_South%2C_1953.png" decoding="async" width="220" height="169" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6a/View_of_Manama_from_the_South%2C_1953.png/330px-View_of_Manama_from_the_South%2C_1953.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6a/View_of_Manama_from_the_South%2C_1953.png/440px-View_of_Manama_from_the_South%2C_1953.png 2x" data-file-width="856" data-file-height="658"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:View_of_Manama_from_the_South,_1953.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div>Overview of Manama, 1953.
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p>After World War II, increasing anti-British sentiment spread throughout the Arab World and led to riots in Bahrain. The riots focused on the Jewish community.<sup id="cite_ref-MOMENT_95-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-MOMENT-95">[95]</a></sup> In 1948, following <a href="/wiki/1947_Manama_pogrom" class="mw-redirect" title="1947 Manama pogrom">rising hostilities and looting</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-israelnationalnews.com_96-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-israelnationalnews.com-96">[96]</a></sup> most members of Bahrain's Jewish community abandoned their properties and evacuated to <a href="/wiki/Bombay" class="mw-redirect" title="Bombay">Bombay</a>, later settling in <a href="/wiki/Israel" title="Israel">Israel</a> (<a href="/wiki/Pardes_Hanna-Karkur" title="Pardes Hanna-Karkur">Pardes Hanna-Karkur</a>) and the United Kingdom. As of 2008<sup class="plainlinks noexcerpt noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Bahrain&amp;action=edit">[update]</a></sup>, 37 Jews remained in the country.<sup id="cite_ref-israelnationalnews.com_96-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-israelnationalnews.com-96">[96]</a></sup> In the 1950s, the <a href="/wiki/National_Union_Committee" title="National Union Committee">National Union Committee</a>, formed by reformists following sectarian clashes, demanded an elected popular assembly, removal of Belgrave and carried out a number of protests and general strikes. In 1965 a month-long <a href="/wiki/March_Intifada" title="March Intifada">uprising</a> broke out after hundreds of workers at the Bahrain Petroleum Company were laid off.<sup id="cite_ref-BBC_11.5.2012_97-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BBC_11.5.2012-97">[97]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Independence">Independence</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Bahrain&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Independence">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:Manama_Souq_1965.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/91/Manama_Souq_1965.JPG/220px-Manama_Souq_1965.JPG" decoding="async" width="220" height="218" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/91/Manama_Souq_1965.JPG/330px-Manama_Souq_1965.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/91/Manama_Souq_1965.JPG/440px-Manama_Souq_1965.JPG 2x" data-file-width="2208" data-file-height="2184"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:Manama_Souq_1965.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div><a href="/wiki/Manama_souq" class="mw-redirect" title="Manama souq">Manama souq</a> in 1965
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p>On 15 August 1971,<sup id="cite_ref-98" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-98">[98]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-99" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-99">[99]</a></sup> though the <a href="/wiki/Shah_of_Iran" class="mw-redirect" title="Shah of Iran">Shah of Iran</a> was claiming historical sovereignty over Bahrain, he accepted <a href="/wiki/Bahraini_independence_survey,_1970" class="mw-redirect" title="Bahraini independence survey, 1970">a referendum</a> held by the United Nations and eventually Bahrain declared independence and signed a new treaty of friendship with the United Kingdom. Bahrain joined the United Nations and the <a href="/wiki/Arab_League" title="Arab League">Arab League</a> later in the year.<sup id="cite_ref-100" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-100">[100]</a></sup> The oil boom of the 1970s benefited Bahrain greatly, although the subsequent downturn hurt the economy. The country had already begun diversification of its economy and benefited further from the <a href="/wiki/Lebanese_Civil_War" title="Lebanese Civil War">Lebanese Civil War</a> in the 1970s and 1980s, when Bahrain replaced <a href="/wiki/Beirut" title="Beirut">Beirut</a> as the Middle East's financial hub after Lebanon's large banking sector was driven out of the country by the war.<sup id="cite_ref-autogenerated3_101-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated3-101">[101]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>Following the 1979 <a href="/wiki/Iranian_Revolution" title="Iranian Revolution">Islamic revolution</a> in Iran in 1981, the Bahraini <a href="/wiki/Shia_Islam" title="Shia Islam">Shia</a> population orchestrated a <a href="/wiki/1981_failed_coup_in_Bahrain" class="mw-redirect" title="1981 failed coup in Bahrain">failed coup</a> attempt under the auspices of a front organisation, the <a href="/wiki/Islamic_Front_for_the_Liberation_of_Bahrain" title="Islamic Front for the Liberation of Bahrain">Islamic Front for the Liberation of Bahrain</a>. The coup would have installed a Shia cleric exiled in Iran, <a href="/wiki/Hujjat_al-Islam" title="Hujjat al-Islam">Hujjatu l-Islām</a> <a href="/wiki/Hadi_al-Modarresi" title="Hadi al-Modarresi">Hādī al-Mudarrisī</a>, as supreme leader heading a <a href="/wiki/Theocracy" title="Theocracy">theocratic government</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-102" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-102">[102]</a></sup> In December 1994, a group of youths threw stones at female runners for running bare-legged during an international marathon. The resulting clash with police soon grew into civil unrest.<sup id="cite_ref-103" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-103">[103]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-104" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-104">[104]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>A <a href="/wiki/1990s_uprising_in_Bahrain" title="1990s uprising in Bahrain">popular uprising</a> occurred between 1994 and 2000 in which leftists, liberals and Islamists joined forces.<sup id="cite_ref-105" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-105">[105]</a></sup> The event resulted in approximately forty deaths and ended after <a href="/wiki/Hamad_bin_Isa_Al_Khalifa" title="Hamad bin Isa Al Khalifa">Hamad bin Isa Al Khalifa</a> became the Emir of Bahrain in 1999.<sup id="cite_ref-106" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-106">[106]</a></sup> He instituted elections for parliament, gave women the right to vote, and released all political prisoners.<sup id="cite_ref-AI_Bahr_promising_107-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-AI_Bahr_promising-107">[107]</a></sup> A referendum on 14–15 February 2001 massively supported the <a href="/wiki/National_Action_Charter_of_Bahrain" title="National Action Charter of Bahrain">National Action Charter</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-UNDP-POGAR_108-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-UNDP-POGAR-108">[108]</a></sup> As part of the adoption of the National Action Charter on 14 February 2002, Bahrain changed its formal name from the State (<i>dawla</i>) of Bahrain to the Kingdom of Bahrain.<sup id="cite_ref-109" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-109">[109]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>The country participated in <a href="/wiki/War_in_Afghanistan_(2001%E2%80%93present)" title="War in Afghanistan (2001–present)">military action</a> against the <a href="/wiki/Taliban" title="Taliban">Taliban</a> in October 2001 by deploying a <a href="/wiki/Frigate" title="Frigate">frigate</a> in the Arabian Sea for rescue and humanitarian operations.<sup id="cite_ref-Europa_110-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Europa-110">[110]</a></sup> As a result, in November of that year, US president <a href="/wiki/George_W._Bush" title="George W. Bush">George W. Bush</a>'s administration designated Bahrain as a "<a href="/wiki/Major_non-NATO_ally" title="Major non-NATO ally">major non-NATO ally</a>".<sup id="cite_ref-Europa_110-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Europa-110">[110]</a></sup> Bahrain opposed the <a href="/wiki/2003_invasion_of_Iraq" title="2003 invasion of Iraq">invasion of Iraq</a> and had offered <a href="/wiki/Saddam_Hussein" title="Saddam Hussein">Saddam Hussein</a> asylum in the days prior to the invasion.<sup id="cite_ref-Europa_110-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Europa-110">[110]</a></sup> Relations improved with neighbouring <a href="/wiki/Qatar" title="Qatar">Qatar</a> after the border dispute over the <a href="/wiki/Hawar_Islands" title="Hawar Islands">Hawar Islands</a> was resolved by the <a href="/wiki/International_Court_of_Justice" title="International Court of Justice">International Court of Justice</a> in <a href="/wiki/The_Hague" title="The Hague">The Hague</a> in 2001. Following the political liberalisation of the country, Bahrain negotiated a <a href="/wiki/Bahrain%E2%80%93United_States_Free_Trade_Agreement" title="Bahrain–United States Free Trade Agreement">free trade agreement</a> with the United States in 2004.<sup id="cite_ref-111" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-111">[111]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h3><span id="Bahraini_protests_2011.E2.80.9313"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Bahraini_protests_2011–13">Bahraini protests 2011–13</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Bahrain&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Bahraini protests 2011–13">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Bahraini_uprising_of_2011" title="Bahraini uprising of 2011">Bahraini uprising of 2011</a>, <a href="/wiki/Saudi-led_intervention_in_Bahrain" title="Saudi-led intervention in Bahrain">Saudi-led intervention in Bahrain</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Arab_Spring" title="Arab Spring">Arab Spring</a>
      </div> 
      <p>Inspired by the regional <a href="/wiki/Arab_Spring" title="Arab Spring">Arab Spring</a>, Bahrain's Shia majority <a href="/wiki/Day_of_Rage_(Bahrain)" title="Day of Rage (Bahrain)">started</a> large protests against its Sunni rulers in early 2011.<sup id="cite_ref-reutbahsoe_112-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-reutbahsoe-112">[112]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-113" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-113">[113]</a></sup> The government initially allowed <a href="/wiki/March_of_loyalty_to_martyrs" title="March of loyalty to martyrs">protests</a> following a <a href="/wiki/Bahrain_Bloody_Thursday" title="Bahrain Bloody Thursday">pre-dawn raid</a> on protesters camped in <a href="/wiki/Pearl_Roundabout" title="Pearl Roundabout">Pearl Roundabout</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-114" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-114">[114]</a></sup> A month later it requested security assistance from <a href="/wiki/Saudi_Arabia" title="Saudi Arabia">Saudi Arabia</a> and other <a href="/wiki/Gulf_Cooperation_Council" title="Gulf Cooperation Council">Gulf Cooperation Council</a> countries and declared a three-month state of emergency.<sup id="cite_ref-bici_115-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bici-115">[115]</a></sup> The government then launched a crackdown on the opposition that included conducting thousands of arrests and <a href="/wiki/Torture_during_the_Bahraini_uprising_(2011%E2%80%93present)" title="Torture during the Bahraini uprising (2011–present)">systematic torture</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-BBC_News_116-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BBC_News-116">[116]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-117" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-117">[117]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-118" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-118">[118]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-aljazeera_119-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-aljazeera-119">[119]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-120" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-120">[120]</a></sup> Almost daily clashes between protesters and security forces led to <a href="/wiki/Casualties_of_the_Bahraini_uprising_(2011%E2%80%93present)" class="mw-redirect" title="Casualties of the Bahraini uprising (2011–present)">dozens of deaths</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-AJE_23.4.2012_121-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-AJE_23.4.2012-121">[121]</a></sup> Protests, sometimes staged by opposition parties, were ongoing.<sup id="cite_ref-reutbahral_122-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-reutbahral-122">[122]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-bbcbahmar_123-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bbcbahmar-123">[123]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-reutbahmass_124-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-reutbahmass-124">[124]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-JSC_live_blog_25_January_2012_125-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-JSC_live_blog_25_January_2012-125">[125]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-JSC_15_February_2012_126-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-JSC_15_February_2012-126">[126]</a></sup> More than 80 civilians and 13 policemen have been killed as of March&nbsp;2014<sup class="plainlinks noexcerpt noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Bahrain&amp;action=edit">[update]</a></sup>.<sup id="cite_ref-127" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-127">[127]</a></sup> According to <a href="/wiki/Physicians_for_Human_Rights" title="Physicians for Human Rights">Physicians for Human Rights</a>, 34 of these deaths were related to government usage of <a href="/wiki/Tear_gas" title="Tear gas">tear gas</a> originally manufactured by U.S.-based <a href="/wiki/Federal_Laboratories" title="Federal Laboratories">Federal Laboratories</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-phr_128-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-phr-128">[128]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-ukforeignaffairscommittee_129-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ukforeignaffairscommittee-129">[129]</a></sup> The lack of coverage by Arab media in the Persian Gulf,<sup id="cite_ref-Hammond_130-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hammond-130">[130]</a></sup> as compared to other <a href="/wiki/Arab_Spring" title="Arab Spring">Arab Spring</a> uprisings, has sparked several controversies. Iran is alleged by <a href="/wiki/United_States" title="United States">United States</a> and others to have a hand in the arming of Bahraini militants.<sup id="cite_ref-131" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-131">[131]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Geography">Geography</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Bahrain&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Geography">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       Main article: <a href="/wiki/Geography_of_Bahrain" title="Geography of Bahrain">Geography of Bahrain</a>
      </div> 
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:Colours_of_the_Persian_Gulf_ESA353290.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c9/Colours_of_the_Persian_Gulf_ESA353290.jpg/220px-Colours_of_the_Persian_Gulf_ESA353290.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="207" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c9/Colours_of_the_Persian_Gulf_ESA353290.jpg/330px-Colours_of_the_Persian_Gulf_ESA353290.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c9/Colours_of_the_Persian_Gulf_ESA353290.jpg/440px-Colours_of_the_Persian_Gulf_ESA353290.jpg 2x" data-file-width="8707" data-file-height="8198"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:Colours_of_the_Persian_Gulf_ESA353290.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div>Satellite view of Bahrain and eastern Saudi Arabia in 2016.
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:Bahrain_map_-_2.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9a/Bahrain_map_-_2.png/220px-Bahrain_map_-_2.png" decoding="async" width="220" height="237" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/9/9a/Bahrain_map_-_2.png 1.5x" data-file-width="328" data-file-height="353"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:Bahrain_map_-_2.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div>Bahrain map 2014
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p>Bahrain is a generally flat and arid <a href="/wiki/Archipelago" title="Archipelago">archipelago</a> in the Persian Gulf. It consists of a low desert plain rising gently to a low central escarpment with the highest point the 134&nbsp;m (440&nbsp;ft) <a href="/wiki/Mountain_of_Smoke" title="Mountain of Smoke">Mountain of Smoke</a> <i>(Jabal ad Dukhan)</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-132" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-132">[132]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Britannica_133-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Britannica-133">[133]</a></sup> Bahrain had a total area of 665&nbsp;km<sup>2</sup> (257&nbsp;sq&nbsp;mi) but due to <a href="/wiki/Land_reclamation" title="Land reclamation">land reclamation</a>, the area increased to 780&nbsp;km<sup>2</sup> (300&nbsp;sq&nbsp;mi), which is slightly larger than <a href="/wiki/Anglesey" title="Anglesey">Anglesey</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Britannica_133-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Britannica-133">[133]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>Often described as an archipelago of 33 islands,<sup id="cite_ref-134" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-134">[134]</a></sup> extensive land reclamation projects have changed this; by August 2008 the number of islands and island groups had increased to 84.<sup id="cite_ref-135" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-135">[135]</a></sup> Bahrain does not share a land boundary with another country but does have a 161&nbsp;km (100&nbsp;mi) coastline. The country also claims a further 22&nbsp;km (12&nbsp;nmi) of <a href="/wiki/Territorial_sea" class="mw-redirect" title="Territorial sea">territorial sea</a> and a 44&nbsp;km (24&nbsp;nmi) <a href="/wiki/Contiguous_zone" class="mw-redirect" title="Contiguous zone">contiguous zone</a>. Bahrain's largest islands are <a href="/wiki/Bahrain_Island" title="Bahrain Island">Bahrain Island</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Hawar_Islands" title="Hawar Islands">Hawar Islands</a>, <a href="/wiki/Muharraq_Island" title="Muharraq Island">Muharraq Island</a>, <a href="/wiki/Umm_an_Nasan" title="Umm an Nasan">Umm an Nasan</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Sitra" title="Sitra">Sitra</a>. Bahrain has mild winters and very hot, humid summers. The country's natural resources include large quantities of oil and natural gas as well as fish in the offshore waters. Arable land constitutes only 2.82%<sup id="cite_ref-CIA_4-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CIA-4">[4]</a></sup> of the total area. </p>
      <p>About 92% of Bahrain is desert with periodic droughts and dust storms, the main natural hazards for Bahrainis.<sup id="cite_ref-TR_136-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-TR-136">[136]</a></sup> Environmental issues facing Bahrain include desertification resulting from the degradation of limited arable land, coastal degradation (damage to coastlines, <a href="/wiki/Coral_reef" title="Coral reef">coral reefs</a>, and sea vegetation) resulting from oil spills and other discharges from large tankers, oil refineries, distribution stations, and illegal land reclamation at places such as <a href="/wiki/Tubli_Bay" title="Tubli Bay">Tubli Bay</a>. The agricultural and domestic sectors' over-utilisation of the Dammam Aquifer, the principal aquifer in Bahrain, has led to its <a href="/wiki/Soil_salinity" title="Soil salinity">salinisation</a> by adjacent brackish and saline water bodies. A hydrochemical study identified the locations of the sources of aquifer salinisation and delineated their areas of influence. The investigation indicates that the aquifer water quality is significantly modified as groundwater flows from the northwestern parts of Bahrain, where the aquifer receives its water by lateral underflow from eastern Saudi Arabia, to the southern and southeastern parts. Four types of salinisation of the aquifer are identified: brackish-water up-flow from the underlying brackish-water zones in north-central, western, and eastern regions; seawater intrusion in the eastern region; intrusion of <a href="/wiki/Sabkha" title="Sabkha">sabkha</a> water in the southwestern region; and irrigation return flow in a local area in the western region. Four alternatives for the management of groundwater quality that are available to the water authorities in Bahrain are discussed and their priority areas are proposed, based on the type and extent of each salinisation source, in addition to groundwater use in that area.<sup id="cite_ref-137" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-137">[137]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Climate">Climate</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Bahrain&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Climate">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       Main article: <a href="/wiki/Climate_of_Bahrain" class="mw-redirect" title="Climate of Bahrain">Climate of Bahrain</a>
      </div> 
      <p>The <a href="/wiki/Zagros_Mountains" title="Zagros Mountains">Zagros Mountains</a> across the Persian Gulf in Iran cause low-level winds to be directed toward Bahrain. Dust storms from Iraq and Saudi Arabia transported by northwesterly winds, locally called <a href="/wiki/Shamal_(wind)" title="Shamal (wind)">shamal</a> wind, causing reduced visibility in the months of June and July.<sup id="cite_ref-138" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-138">[138]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>Summers are very hot. The seas around Bahrain are very shallow, heating up quickly in the summer to produce very high <a href="/wiki/Humidity" title="Humidity">humidity</a>, especially at night. Summer temperatures may reach up to 50&nbsp;°C (122&nbsp;°F) under the right conditions.<sup id="cite_ref-139" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-139">[139]</a></sup> Rainfall in Bahrain is minimal and irregular. Precipitation mostly occurs in winter, with an average of 70.8mm of rainfall recorded annually. </p>
