       </tbody>
      </table> 
      <div style="clear:both;"></div> 
      <p>The Central Governorate was abolished in September 2014, its territory divided between the <a href="/wiki/Northern_Governorate" title="Northern Governorate">Northern Governorate</a>, <a href="/wiki/Southern_Governorate" title="Southern Governorate">Southern Governorate</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Capital_Governorate,_Bahrain" title="Capital Governorate, Bahrain">Capital Governorate</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-229" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-229">[229]</a></sup> </p> 
      <table class="wikitable"> 
       <tbody>
        <tr> 
         <th>Map</th> 
         <th>Current Governorates </th>
        </tr> 
        <tr> 
         <td rowspan="4"><a href="/wiki/File:New_Governorates_of_Bahrain_2014.svg" class="image"><img alt="New Governorates of Bahrain 2014.svg" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5e/New_Governorates_of_Bahrain_2014.svg/256px-New_Governorates_of_Bahrain_2014.svg.png" decoding="async" width="256" height="364" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5e/New_Governorates_of_Bahrain_2014.svg/384px-New_Governorates_of_Bahrain_2014.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5e/New_Governorates_of_Bahrain_2014.svg/512px-New_Governorates_of_Bahrain_2014.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="300" data-file-height="426"></a></td> 
         <td><span style="background:yellow"> 1</span> – <b><a href="/wiki/Capital_Governorate,_Bahrain" title="Capital Governorate, Bahrain">Capital Governorate</a></b> </td>
        </tr> 
        <tr> 
         <td><span style="background:#F0DC82"> 2</span> – <b><a href="/wiki/Muharraq_Governorate" title="Muharraq Governorate">Muharraq Governorate</a></b> </td>
        </tr> 
        <tr> 
         <td><span style="background:#7bf;"> 3</span> – <b><a href="/wiki/Northern_Governorate" title="Northern Governorate">Northern Governorate</a></b> </td>
        </tr> 
        <tr> 
         <td><span style="background:pink"> 4</span> – <b><a href="/wiki/Southern_Governorate" title="Southern Governorate">Southern Governorate</a></b> </td>
        </tr>
       </tbody>
      </table> 
      <p>The United States designated Bahrain a <a href="/wiki/Major_non-NATO_ally" title="Major non-NATO ally">major non-NATO ally</a> in 2001.<sup id="cite_ref-230" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-230">[230]</a></sup> As of October&nbsp;2014<sup class="plainlinks noexcerpt noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Bahrain&amp;action=edit">[update]</a></sup>, Bahrain is ruled by an "<a href="/wiki/Authoritarian_regime" class="mw-redirect" title="Authoritarian regime">authoritarian regime</a>" and is rated as "Not Free" by the U.S.-based non-governmental <a href="/wiki/Freedom_House" title="Freedom House">Freedom House</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FH2013_231-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FH2013-231">[231]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Economy">Economy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Bahrain&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Economy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       Main article: <a href="/wiki/Economy_of_Bahrain" title="Economy of Bahrain">Economy of Bahrain</a>
      </div> 
      <div class="thumb tnone" style="margin-left:auto;margin-right:auto;overflow:hidden;width:auto;max-width:908px">
       <div class="thumbinner">
        <div class="noresize" style="overflow:auto">
         <a href="/wiki/File:Manama_Skyline.jpg" class="image" title="The skyline of Manama, Bahrain"><img alt="Manama cityline" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c0/Manama_Skyline.jpg/900px-Manama_Skyline.jpg" decoding="async" width="900" height="157" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c0/Manama_Skyline.jpg/1350px-Manama_Skyline.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c0/Manama_Skyline.jpg/1800px-Manama_Skyline.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3888" data-file-height="677"></a>
        </div>
        <div class="thumbcaption" style="text-align:center">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:Manama_Skyline.jpg" title="File:Manama Skyline.jpg"> </a>
         </div>The skyline of <a href="/wiki/Manama" title="Manama">Manama</a>, Bahrain
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <table class="box-Update plainlinks metadata ambox ambox-content ambox-Update" role="presentation">
       <tbody>
        <tr>
         <td class="mbox-image">
          <div style="width:52px">
           <img alt="Ambox current red Asia Australia.svg" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bd/Ambox_current_red_Asia_Australia.svg/42px-Ambox_current_red_Asia_Australia.svg.png" decoding="async" width="42" height="34" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bd/Ambox_current_red_Asia_Australia.svg/63px-Ambox_current_red_Asia_Australia.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bd/Ambox_current_red_Asia_Australia.svg/84px-Ambox_current_red_Asia_Australia.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="360" data-file-height="290">
          </div></td>
         <td class="mbox-text">
          <div class="mbox-text-span">
           This article needs to be <b>updated</b>.<span class="hide-when-compact"> Please update this section to reflect recent events or newly available information.</span> <small class="date-container"><i>(<span class="date">November 2020</span>)</i></small>
          </div></td>
        </tr>
       </tbody>
      </table> 
      <p>According to a January 2006 report by the <a href="/wiki/United_Nations_Economic_and_Social_Commission_for_Western_Asia" title="United Nations Economic and Social Commission for Western Asia">United Nations Economic and Social Commission for Western Asia</a>, Bahrain has the fastest-growing economy in the Arab world.<sup id="cite_ref-Ref_2008_232-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ref_2008-232">[232]</a></sup> Bahrain also has the freest economy in the Middle East and is twelfth-freest overall in the world based on the 2011 <a href="/wiki/Index_of_Economic_Freedom" title="Index of Economic Freedom">Index of Economic Freedom</a> published by the <a href="/wiki/Heritage_Foundation" class="mw-redirect" title="Heritage Foundation">Heritage Foundation</a>/<i><a href="/wiki/The_Wall_Street_Journal" title="The Wall Street Journal">The Wall Street Journal</a></i>.<sup id="cite_ref-Ref_2011_233-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ref_2011-233">[233]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>In 2008, Bahrain was named the world's fastest-growing financial center by the City of London's <a href="/wiki/Global_Financial_Centres_Index" title="Global Financial Centres Index">Global Financial Centres Index</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-234" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-234">[234]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-autogenerated1_235-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated1-235">[235]</a></sup> Bahrain's banking and financial services sector, particularly <a href="/wiki/Islamic_banking" class="mw-redirect" title="Islamic banking">Islamic banking</a>, have benefited from the regional boom driven by demand for oil.<sup id="cite_ref-autogenerated5_236-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated5-236">[236]</a></sup> Petroleum production and processing is Bahrain's most exported product, accounting for 60% of export receipts, 70% of government revenues, and 11% of <a href="/wiki/Gross_domestic_product" title="Gross domestic product">GDP</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-CIA_4-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CIA-4">[4]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Aluminium_Bahrain" title="Aluminium Bahrain">Aluminium production</a> is the second-most exported product, followed by finance and construction materials.<sup id="cite_ref-CIA_4-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CIA-4">[4]</a></sup> </p> 
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:Manama,_Bahrain_Decembre_2014.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2d/Manama%2C_Bahrain_Decembre_2014.jpg/220px-Manama%2C_Bahrain_Decembre_2014.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="147" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2d/Manama%2C_Bahrain_Decembre_2014.jpg/330px-Manama%2C_Bahrain_Decembre_2014.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2d/Manama%2C_Bahrain_Decembre_2014.jpg/440px-Manama%2C_Bahrain_Decembre_2014.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1600" data-file-height="1067"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:Manama,_Bahrain_Decembre_2014.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div>Manama skyline as viewed from <a href="/wiki/Juffair" title="Juffair">Juffair</a>
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p>Economic conditions have fluctuated with the changing price of oil since 1985, for example during and following the <a href="/wiki/Gulf_War" title="Gulf War">Persian Gulf crisis of 1990–91</a>. With its highly developed communication and transport facilities, Bahrain is home to a number of multinational firms and construction proceeds on several major industrial projects. A large share of exports consist of petroleum products made from imported crude oil, which accounted for 51% of the country's imports in 2007.<sup id="cite_ref-TR_136-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-TR-136">[136]</a></sup> Bahrain depends heavily on food imports to feed its growing population; it relies heavily on meat imports from <a href="/wiki/Australia" title="Australia">Australia</a> and also imports 75% of its total fruit consumption needs.<sup id="cite_ref-237" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-237">[237]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-DT_238-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-DT-238">[238]</a></sup> Since only 2.9% of the country's land is <a href="/wiki/Arable_land" title="Arable land">arable</a>, <a href="/wiki/Agriculture" title="Agriculture">agriculture</a> contributes to 0.5% of Bahrain's GDP.<sup id="cite_ref-DT_238-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-DT-238">[238]</a></sup> In 2004, Bahrain signed the <a href="/wiki/Bahrain%E2%80%93United_States_Free_Trade_Agreement" title="Bahrain–United States Free Trade Agreement">Bahrain–US Free Trade Agreement</a>, which will reduce certain trade barriers between the two nations.<sup id="cite_ref-autogenerated8_239-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated8-239">[239]</a></sup> In 2011, due to the combination of the <a href="/wiki/Global_financial_crisis" class="mw-redirect" title="Global financial crisis">global financial crisis</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Bahraini_uprising_(2011%E2%80%93present)" class="mw-redirect" title="Bahraini uprising (2011–present)">recent unrest</a>, the gdp growth rate decreased to 1.3%, which was the lowest growth rate since 1994.<sup id="cite_ref-240" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-240">[240]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>Access to <a href="/wiki/Biocapacity" title="Biocapacity">biocapacity</a> in Bahrain is much lower than world average. In 2016, Bahrain had 0.52 global hectares <sup id="cite_ref-footprintdata_241-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-footprintdata-241">[241]</a></sup> of biocapacity per person within its territory, much less than the world average of 1.6 global hectares per person.<sup id="cite_ref-242" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-242">[242]</a></sup> In 2016 Bahrain used 8.6 global hectares of biocapacity per person - their <a href="/wiki/Ecological_footprint" title="Ecological footprint">ecological footprint</a> of consumption. This means they use 16.5 times as much biocapacity as Bahrain contains. As a result, Bahrain is running a biocapacity deficit.<sup id="cite_ref-footprintdata_241-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-footprintdata-241">[241]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>Unemployment, especially among the young, and the depletion of both oil and underground water resources are major long-term economic problems. In 2008, the jobless figure was at 4%,<sup id="cite_ref-Ref_2008a_243-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ref_2008a-243">[243]</a></sup> with women over represented at 85% of the total.<sup id="cite_ref-Ref_2008b_244-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ref_2008b-244">[244]</a></sup> In 2007 Bahrain became the first Arab country to institute <a href="/wiki/Unemployment_benefits" title="Unemployment benefits">unemployment benefits</a> as part of a series of labour reforms instigated under Minister of Labour, Dr. <a href="/wiki/Majeed_Al_Alawi" title="Majeed Al Alawi">Majeed Al Alawi</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-245" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-245">[245]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Tourism">Tourism</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Bahrain&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Tourism">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       Main article: <a href="/wiki/Tourism_in_Bahrain" title="Tourism in Bahrain">Tourism in Bahrain</a>
      </div> 
      <div class="thumb tleft">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:Muharraq_and_Manama.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1b/Muharraq_and_Manama.jpg/220px-Muharraq_and_Manama.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="109" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1b/Muharraq_and_Manama.jpg/330px-Muharraq_and_Manama.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1b/Muharraq_and_Manama.jpg/440px-Muharraq_and_Manama.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2304" data-file-height="1137"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:Muharraq_and_Manama.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div>The cities of <a href="/wiki/Muharraq" title="Muharraq">Muharraq</a> (<i>foreground</i>) and <a href="/wiki/Manama" title="Manama">Manama</a> (background)
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p>As a tourist destination, Bahrain received over eight million visitors in 2008.<sup id="cite_ref-246" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-246">[246]</a></sup> Most of these are from the surrounding Arab states although an increasing number hail from outside the region due to growing awareness of the kingdom's heritage and its higher profile as a result of the <a href="/wiki/Bahrain_Grand_Prix" title="Bahrain Grand Prix">Bahrain International F1 Circuit</a>. </p>
      <p>The kingdom combines modern Arab culture and the archaeological legacy of five thousand years of civilisation. The island is home to forts including <a href="/wiki/Qalat_Al_Bahrain" class="mw-redirect" title="Qalat Al Bahrain">Qalat Al Bahrain</a> which has been listed by <a href="/wiki/UNESCO" title="UNESCO">UNESCO</a> as a <a href="/wiki/World_Heritage_Site" title="World Heritage Site">World Heritage Site</a>. The <a href="/wiki/Bahrain_National_Museum" title="Bahrain National Museum">Bahrain National Museum</a> has artefacts from the country's history dating back to the island's first human inhabitants some 9000 years ago and the <a href="/wiki/Beit_Al_Quran" title="Beit Al Quran">Beit Al Quran</a> (Arabic: بيت القرآن, meaning: the House of Qur'an) is a museum that holds Islamic artefacts of the <a href="/wiki/Qur%27an" class="mw-redirect" title="Qur'an">Qur'an</a>. Some of the popular historical tourist attractions in the kingdom are the <a href="/wiki/Al_Khamis_Mosque" class="mw-redirect" title="Al Khamis Mosque">Al Khamis Mosque</a>, which is one of the oldest mosques in the region, the <a href="/wiki/Arad_fort" class="mw-redirect" title="Arad fort">Arad fort</a> in Muharraq, <a href="/wiki/Barbar_temple" class="mw-redirect" title="Barbar temple">Barbar temple</a>, which is an ancient temple from the Dilmunite period of Bahrain, as well as the <a href="/wiki/Dilmun_Burial_Mounds" title="Dilmun Burial Mounds">A'ali Burial Mounds</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Sar,_Bahrain" title="Sar, Bahrain">Saar</a> temple.<sup id="cite_ref-247" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-247">[247]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Tree_of_Life,_Bahrain" class="mw-redirect" title="Tree of Life, Bahrain">Tree of Life</a>, a 400-year-old tree that grows in the <a href="/wiki/Sakhir" title="Sakhir">Sakhir</a> desert with no nearby water, is also a popular tourist attraction.<sup id="cite_ref-248" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-248">[248]</a></sup> </p> 
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:TreeofLife.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a3/TreeofLife.JPG/220px-TreeofLife.JPG" decoding="async" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a3/TreeofLife.JPG/330px-TreeofLife.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a3/TreeofLife.JPG/440px-TreeofLife.JPG 2x" data-file-width="2304" data-file-height="1728"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:TreeofLife.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div>The <a href="/wiki/Tree_of_Life,_Bahrain" class="mw-redirect" title="Tree of Life, Bahrain">Tree of Life</a>
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p><a href="/wiki/Bird_watching" class="mw-redirect" title="Bird watching">Bird watching</a> (primarily in the <a href="/wiki/Hawar_Islands" title="Hawar Islands">Hawar Islands</a>), <a href="/wiki/Scuba_diving" title="Scuba diving">scuba diving</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Horse_riding" class="mw-redirect" title="Horse riding">horse riding</a> are popular tourist activities in Bahrain. Many tourists from nearby Saudi Arabia and across the region visit Manama primarily for the <a href="/wiki/Shopping_malls" class="mw-redirect" title="Shopping malls">shopping malls</a> in the capital Manama, such as the <a href="/wiki/Bahrain_City_Centre" class="mw-redirect" title="Bahrain City Centre">Bahrain City Centre</a> and <a href="/wiki/Seef_Mall" title="Seef Mall">Seef Mall</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Seef" title="Seef">Seef</a> district of Manama. The <a href="/wiki/Manama_Souq" title="Manama Souq">Manama Souq</a> and <i>Gold Souq</i> in the old district of <a href="/wiki/Manama" title="Manama">Manama</a> are also popular with tourists.<sup id="cite_ref-MoFA_249-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-MoFA-249">[249]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>In January 2019 the state-run Bahrain News Agency announced the summer 2019 opening of an underwater theme park covering about 100,000 square meters with a sunken <a href="/wiki/Boeing_747" title="Boeing 747">Boeing 747</a> as the site's centerpiece. The project is a partnership between the Supreme Council for Environment, Bahrain Tourism and Exhibitions Authority (BTEA), and private investors. Bahrain hopes scuba divers from around the world will visit the underwater park, which will also include artificial <a href="/wiki/Coral_reef" title="Coral reef">coral reefs</a>, a copy of a Bahraini pearl merchant's house, and sculptures.<sup id="cite_ref-250" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-250">[250]</a></sup> The park is intended to become the world's largest eco-friendly underwater theme park.<sup id="cite_ref-251" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-251">[251]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>Since 2005, Bahrain hosts an annual festival in March, titled <i>Spring of Culture</i>, which features internationally renowned musicians and artists performing in concerts.<sup id="cite_ref-TA_252-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-TA-252">[252]</a></sup> Manama was named the <a href="/wiki/Arab_Capital_of_Culture" title="Arab Capital of Culture">Arab Capital of Culture</a> for 2012 and <i>Capital of Arab Tourism</i> for 2013 by the Arab League and Asian Tourism for 2014 with the Gulf Capital of Tourism for 2016 by The Gulf Cooperation Council. The 2012 festival featured concerts starring <a href="/wiki/Andrea_Bocelli" title="Andrea Bocelli">Andrea Bocelli</a>, <a href="/wiki/Julio_Iglesias" title="Julio Iglesias">Julio Iglesias</a> and other musicians.<sup id="cite_ref-TOB_253-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-TOB-253">[253]</a></sup> </p> 
      <div style="clear:both;"></div> 
      <h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Infrastructure">Infrastructure</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Bahrain&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Infrastructure">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       Main article: <a href="/wiki/Transport_in_Bahrain" title="Transport in Bahrain">Transport in Bahrain</a>
      </div> 
      <p>Bahrain has one main <a href="/wiki/International_airport" title="International airport">international airport</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Bahrain_International_Airport" title="Bahrain International Airport">Bahrain International Airport</a> (BAH) which is located on the island of <a href="/wiki/Muharraq_Island" title="Muharraq Island">Muharraq</a>, in the north-east. The airport handled more than 100,000 flights and more than 8 million passengers in 2010.<sup id="cite_ref-254" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-254">[254]</a></sup> Bahrain's national carrier, <a href="/wiki/Gulf_Air" title="Gulf Air">Gulf Air</a> operates and bases itself in the BIA. </p> 
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:King_Fahd_causeway_satellite.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ab/King_Fahd_causeway_satellite.png/220px-King_Fahd_causeway_satellite.png" decoding="async" width="220" height="145" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ab/King_Fahd_causeway_satellite.png/330px-King_Fahd_causeway_satellite.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ab/King_Fahd_causeway_satellite.png/440px-King_Fahd_causeway_satellite.png 2x" data-file-width="992" data-file-height="654"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:King_Fahd_causeway_satellite.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div>The King Fahd Causeway as seen from space
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p>Bahrain has a well-developed <a href="/wiki/Road_transport_in_Bahrain" class="mw-redirect" title="Road transport in Bahrain">road network</a>, particularly in Manama. The discovery of oil in the early 1930s accelerated the creation of multiple roads and <a href="/wiki/Highways" class="mw-redirect" title="Highways">highways</a> in Bahrain, connecting several isolated villages, such as <a href="/wiki/Budaiya" title="Budaiya">Budaiya</a>, to Manama.<sup id="cite_ref-Yasser_255-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Yasser-255">[255]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>To the east, a bridge connected <a href="/wiki/Manama" title="Manama">Manama</a> to <a href="/wiki/Muharraq_Island" title="Muharraq Island">Muharraq</a> since 1929, a new causeway was built in 1941 which replaced the old wooden bridge.<sup id="cite_ref-Yasser_255-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Yasser-255">[255]</a></sup> Currently there are three modern bridges connecting the two locations.<sup id="cite_ref-256" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-256">[256]</a></sup> Transits between the two islands peaked after the construction of the Bahrain International Airport in 1932.<sup id="cite_ref-Yasser_255-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Yasser-255">[255]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Ring_roads" class="mw-redirect" title="Ring roads">Ring roads</a> and highways were later built to connect Manama to the villages of the <a href="/wiki/Northern_Governorate" title="Northern Governorate">Northern Governorate</a> and towards towns in central and southern Bahrain. </p>
      <p>The four main islands and all the towns and villages are linked by well-constructed roads. There were 3,164&nbsp;km (1,966&nbsp;mi) of roadways in 2002, of which 2,433&nbsp;km (1,512&nbsp;mi) were paved. A <a href="/wiki/Causeway" title="Causeway">causeway</a> stretching over 2.8&nbsp;km (2&nbsp;mi), connect Manama with <a href="/wiki/Muharraq_Island" title="Muharraq Island">Muharraq Island</a>, and another bridge joins <a href="/wiki/Sitra" title="Sitra">Sitra</a> to the main island. The <a href="/wiki/King_Fahd_Causeway" title="King Fahd Causeway">King Fahd Causeway</a>, measuring 24&nbsp;km (15&nbsp;mi), links Bahrain with the Saudi Arabian mainland via the island of <a href="/wiki/Umm_an-Nasan" class="mw-redirect" title="Umm an-Nasan">Umm an-Nasan</a>. It was completed in December 1986, and financed by <a href="/wiki/Saudi_Arabia" title="Saudi Arabia">Saudi Arabia</a>. In 2008, there were 17,743,495 passengers transiting through the causeway.<sup id="cite_ref-257" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-257">[257]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>Bahrain's port of <a href="/wiki/Mina_Salman" title="Mina Salman">Mina Salman</a> is the main <a href="/wiki/Seaport" class="mw-redirect" title="Seaport">seaport</a> of the country and consists of 15 <a href="/wiki/Berth_(moorings)" title="Berth (moorings)">berths</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-autogenerated9_258-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated9-258">[258]</a></sup> In 2001, Bahrain had a merchant fleet of eight ships of 1,000 <a href="/wiki/Gross_Tonnage" class="mw-redirect" title="Gross Tonnage">GT</a> or over, totaling 270,784 GT.<sup id="cite_ref-259" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-259">[259]</a></sup> Private vehicles and taxis are the primary means of transportation in the city.<sup id="cite_ref-260" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-260">[260]</a></sup> A <a href="/wiki/Bahrain_Metro" class="mw-redirect" title="Bahrain Metro">nationwide metro system</a> is currently under construction and is due to be operational by 2023. </p> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Telecommunications">Telecommunications</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Bahrain&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Telecommunications">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Telecommunications_in_Bahrain" title="Telecommunications in Bahrain">Telecommunications in Bahrain</a> and <a href="/wiki/Internet_in_Bahrain" title="Internet in Bahrain">Internet in Bahrain</a>
      </div> 
      <p>The <a href="/wiki/Telecommunications" class="mw-redirect" title="Telecommunications">telecommunications</a> sector in Bahrain officially started in 1981 with the establishment of Bahrain's first telecommunications company, <a href="/wiki/Batelco" title="Batelco">Batelco</a> and until 2004, it <a href="/wiki/Monopoly" title="Monopoly">monopolised</a> the sector. In 1981, there were more than 45,000 telephones in use in the country. By 1999, Batelco had more than 100,000 mobile contracts.<sup id="cite_ref-RE_261-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-RE-261">[261]</a></sup> In 2002, under pressure from international bodies, Bahrain implemented its telecommunications law which included the establishment of an independent <i><a href="/wiki/Telecommunications_Regulatory_Authority_of_Bahrain" title="Telecommunications Regulatory Authority of Bahrain">Telecommunications Regulatory Authority</a></i> (TRA).<sup id="cite_ref-RE_261-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-RE-261">[261]</a></sup> In 2004, <a href="/wiki/Zain_Group" title="Zain Group">Zain</a> (a rebranded version of <a href="/wiki/MTC_Vodafone" class="mw-redirect" title="MTC Vodafone">MTC Vodafone</a>) started operations in Bahrain and in 2010 <a href="/wiki/VIVA_Bahrain" class="mw-redirect" title="VIVA Bahrain">VIVA</a> (owned by <a href="/wiki/Saudi_Telecom_Company" title="Saudi Telecom Company">STC</a> Group) became the third company to provide mobile services.<sup id="cite_ref-262" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-262">[262]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>Bahrain has been connected to the internet since 1995 with the country's <a href="/wiki/Domain_suffix" class="mw-redirect" title="Domain suffix">domain suffix</a> is '<a href="/wiki/.bh" title=".bh">.bh</a>'. The country's connectivity score (a statistic which measures both Internet access and fixed and mobile telephone lines) is 210.4 percent per person, while the regional average in <a href="/wiki/Arab_States_of_the_Persian_Gulf" class="mw-redirect" title="Arab States of the Persian Gulf">Arab States of the Persian Gulf</a> is 135.37 percent.<sup id="cite_ref-263" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-263">[263]</a></sup> The number of Bahraini <a href="/wiki/Internet_users" class="mw-redirect" title="Internet users">internet users</a> has risen from 40,000 in 2000<sup id="cite_ref-264" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-264">[264]</a></sup> to 250,000 in 2008,<sup id="cite_ref-265" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-265">[265]</a></sup> or from 5.95 to 33 percent of the population. As of August&nbsp;2013<sup class="plainlinks noexcerpt noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Bahrain&amp;action=edit">[update]</a></sup>, the TRA has licensed 22 <a href="/wiki/Internet_Service_Providers" class="mw-redirect" title="Internet Service Providers">Internet Service Providers</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-266" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-266">[266]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Science_and_technology">Science and technology</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Bahrain&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Science and technology">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Policy_framework">Policy framework</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Bahrain&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: Policy framework">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
      <p>The <i>Bahraini Economic Vision 2030</i> published in 2008 does not indicate how the stated goal of shifting from an economy built on oil wealth to a productive, globally competitive economy will be attained. Bahrain has already diversified its exports to some extent, out of necessity. It has the smallest hydrocarbon reserves of any Persian Gulf state, producing 48,000 barrels per day from its one onshore field.<sup id="cite_ref-267" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-267">[267]</a></sup> The bulk of the country's revenue comes from its share in the offshore field administered by Saudi Arabia. The gas reserve in Bahrain is expected to last for less than 27 years, leaving the country with few sources of capital to pursue the development of new industries. Investment in research and development remained very low in 2013.<sup id="cite_ref-:0_268-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:0-268">[268]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>Apart from the Ministry of Education and the Higher Education Council, the two main hives of activity in science, technology, and innovation are the University of Bahrain (established in 1986) and the Bahrain Centre for Strategic, International, and Energy Studies. The latter was founded in 2009 to undertake research with a focus on strategic security and energy issues to encourage new thinking and influence policy-making.<sup id="cite_ref-:0_268-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:0-268">[268]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="New_infrastructure_for_science_and_education">New infrastructure for science and education</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Bahrain&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: New infrastructure for science and education">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
      <p>Bahrain hopes to build a science culture within the kingdom and to encourage technological innovation, among other goals. In 2013, the Bahrain Science Centre was launched as an interactive educational facility targeting 6- to 18-year-olds. The topics covered by current exhibitions include junior engineering, human health, the five senses, Earth sciences and biodiversity.<sup id="cite_ref-:0_268-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:0-268">[268]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>In April 2014, Bahrain launched its National Space Science Agency. The agency has been working to ratify international space-related agreements such as the Outer Space Treaty, the Rescue Agreement, the Space Liability Convention, the Registration Convention and the Moon Agreement. The agency plans to establish infrastructure for the observation of both outer space and the Earth.<sup id="cite_ref-:0_268-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:0-268">[268]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>In November 2008, an agreement was signed to establish a Regional Centre for Information and Communication Technology in Manama under the auspices of UNESCO. The aim is to establish a knowledge hub for the six member states of the Gulf Cooperation Council. In March 2012, the centre hosted two high-level workshops on ICTs and education. In 2013, Bahrain topped the Arab world for internet penetration (90% of the population), trailed by the United Arab Emirates (86%) and Qatar (85%). Just half of Bahrainis and Qataris (53%) and two-thirds of those in the United Arab Emirates (64%) had access in 2009.<sup id="cite_ref-:0_268-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:0-268">[268]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Investment_in_education_and_research">Investment in education and research</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Bahrain&amp;action=edit&amp;section=27" title="Edit section: Investment in education and research">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
      <p>In 2012, the government devoted 2.6% of GDP to education, one of the lowest ratios in the Arab world. This ratio was on a par with investment in education in Lebanon and higher only than that in Qatar (2.4% in 2008) and Sudan (2.2% in 2009).<sup id="cite_ref-:0_268-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:0-268">[268]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>Bahrain invests little in research and development. In 2009 and 2013, this investment reportedly amounted to 0.04% of GDP, although the data were incomplete, covering only the higher education sector. The lack of comprehensive data on research and development poses a challenge for policy-makers, as data inform evidence-based policy-making.<sup id="cite_ref-:0_268-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:0-268">[268]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>The available data for researchers in 2013 only cover the higher education sector. Here, the number of researchers is equivalent to 50 per million inhabitants, compared to a global average for all employment sectors of 1,083 per million.<sup id="cite_ref-:0_268-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:0-268">[268]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>The <a href="/wiki/University_of_Bahrain" title="University of Bahrain">University of Bahrain</a> had over 20,000 students in 2014, 65% of whom are women, and around 900 faculty members, 40% of whom are women. From 1986 to 2014, university staff published 5 500 papers and books. The university spent about US$11 million per year on research in 2014, which was conducted by a contingent of 172 men and 128 women. Women thus made up 43% of researchers at the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Bahrain" title="University of Bahrain">University of Bahrain</a> in 2014.<sup id="cite_ref-:0_268-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:0-268">[268]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>Bahrain was one of 11 Arab states which counted a majority of female university graduates in science and engineering in 2014. Women accounted for 66% of graduates in natural sciences, 28% of those in engineering and 77% of those in health and welfare. It is harder to judge the contribution of women to research, as the data for 2013 only cover the higher education sector.<sup id="cite_ref-:0_268-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:0-268">[268]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Trends_in_research_output">Trends in research output</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Bahrain&amp;action=edit&amp;section=28" title="Edit section: Trends in research output">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
      <p>In 2014, Bahraini scientists published 155 articles in internationally cataloged journals, according to Thomson Reuters' Web of Science (Science Citation Index Expanded). This corresponds to 15 articles per million inhabitants, compared to a global average of 176 per million inhabitants in 2013. Scientific output has risen slowly from 93 articles in 2005 and remains modest. By 2014, only Mauritania and Palestine had a smaller output in this database among Arab states.<sup id="cite_ref-269" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-269">[269]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-:0_268-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:0-268">[268]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>Between 2008 and 2014, Bahraini scientists collaborated most with their peers from Saudi Arabia (137 articles), followed by Egypt (101), the United Kingdom (93), the United States (89) and Tunisia (75).<sup id="cite_ref-:0_268-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:0-268">[268]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Demographics">Demographics</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Bahrain&amp;action=edit&amp;section=29" title="Edit section: Demographics">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Demographics_of_Bahrain" title="Demographics of Bahrain">Demographics of Bahrain</a> and <a href="/wiki/Freedom_of_religion_in_Bahrain" title="Freedom of religion in Bahrain">Freedom of religion in Bahrain</a>
      </div> 
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:Joining_in_prayer_-_Flickr_-_Al_Jazeera_English.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/aa/Joining_in_prayer_-_Flickr_-_Al_Jazeera_English.jpg/220px-Joining_in_prayer_-_Flickr_-_Al_Jazeera_English.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="124" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/aa/Joining_in_prayer_-_Flickr_-_Al_Jazeera_English.jpg/330px-Joining_in_prayer_-_Flickr_-_Al_Jazeera_English.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/aa/Joining_in_prayer_-_Flickr_-_Al_Jazeera_English.jpg/440px-Joining_in_prayer_-_Flickr_-_Al_Jazeera_English.jpg 2x" data-file-width="4000" data-file-height="2248"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:Joining_in_prayer_-_Flickr_-_Al_Jazeera_English.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div>Bahrainis observing public prayers in Manama
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p>In 2010, Bahrain's population grew to 1.2 million, of which 568,399 were Bahraini and 666,172 were non-nationals.<sup id="cite_ref-2010-census_12-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-2010-census-12">[12]</a></sup> It had risen from 1.05 million (517,368 non-nationals) in 2007, the year when Bahrain's population crossed the one million mark.<sup id="cite_ref-Ref_2008c_270-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ref_2008c-270">[270]</a></sup> Though a majority of the population is Middle Eastern, a sizeable number of people from South Asia live in the country. In 2008, approximately 290,000 <a href="/wiki/Non-resident_Indian_and_person_of_Indian_origin" title="Non-resident Indian and person of Indian origin">Indian nationals</a> lived in Bahrain, making them the single largest expatriate community in the country, the majority of which hail from the south Indian state of <a href="/wiki/Kerala" title="Kerala">Kerala</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Ref_2008d_271-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ref_2008d-271">[271]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-IndEmb_272-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-IndEmb-272">[272]</a></sup> Bahrain is the fourth <a href="/wiki/List_of_sovereign_states_and_dependent_territories_by_population_density" class="mw-redirect" title="List of sovereign states and dependent territories by population density">most densely populated sovereign state in the world</a> with a population density of 1,646 people per km<sup>2</sup> in 2010.<sup id="cite_ref-2010-census_12-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-2010-census-12">[12]</a></sup> The only sovereign states with larger population densities are <a href="/wiki/City_states" class="mw-redirect" title="City states">city states</a>. Much of this population is concentrated in the north of the country with the <a href="/wiki/Southern_Governorate" title="Southern Governorate">Southern Governorate</a> being the least densely populated part.<sup id="cite_ref-2010-census_12-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-2010-census-12">[12]</a></sup> The north of the country is so urbanised that it is considered by some to be one large <a href="/wiki/Metropolitan_area" title="Metropolitan area">metropolitan area</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-273" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-273">[273]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Ethnic_groups">Ethnic groups</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Bahrain&amp;action=edit&amp;section=30" title="Edit section: Ethnic groups">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Bahraini_people" class="mw-redirect" title="Bahraini people">Bahraini people</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ethnic,_cultural_and_religious_groups_of_Bahrain" title="Ethnic, cultural and religious groups of Bahrain">Ethnic, cultural and religious groups of Bahrain</a>
      </div> 
      <p>Bahraini people are ethnically diverse. Shia Bahrainis are divided into two main ethnic groups: <a href="/wiki/Baharna" class="mw-redirect" title="Baharna">Baharna</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ajam_of_Bahrain" title="Ajam of Bahrain">Ajam</a>. The Shia Bahrainis are <a href="/wiki/Baharna" class="mw-redirect" title="Baharna">Baharna</a> (Arab), and the <a href="/wiki/Ajam_of_Bahrain" title="Ajam of Bahrain">Ajam</a> are <a href="/wiki/Iranian_people" class="mw-redirect" title="Iranian people">Persian</a> Shias. Shia Persians form large communities in Manama and Muharraq. A small minority of Shia Bahrainis are ethnic Hasawis from <a href="/wiki/Al-Ahsa_Oasis" title="Al-Ahsa Oasis">Al-Hasa</a>. </p>
      <p>Sunni Bahrainis are mainly divided into two main ethnic groups: Arabs (al Arab) and <a href="/wiki/Huwala" class="mw-redirect" title="Huwala">Huwala</a>. Sunni Arabs, while a minority, are the most influential ethnic group in Bahrain. They hold most government positions and the <a href="/wiki/Al_Khalifa" class="mw-redirect" title="Al Khalifa">Bahraini monarchy</a> are Sunni Arabs. Sunni Arabs have traditionally lived in areas such as Zallaq, Muharraq, Riffa and Hawar islands. The Huwala are descendants of Sunni Iranians; some of them are Sunni Persians,<sup id="cite_ref-mig_274-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-mig-274">[274]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-275" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-275">[275]</a></sup> while others Sunni Arabs.<sup id="cite_ref-276" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-276">[276]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-277" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-277">[277]</a></sup> There are also Sunnis of <a href="/wiki/Baloch_people" title="Baloch people">Baloch</a> origin. Most African Bahrainis come from <a href="/wiki/East_Africa" title="East Africa">East Africa</a> and have traditionally lived in Muharraq Island and Riffa.<sup id="cite_ref-278" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-278">[278]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Religion">Religion</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Bahrain&amp;action=edit&amp;section=31" title="Edit section: Religion">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>

       </div> 
      </div> 
      <p>The state religion of Bahrain is Islam and most Bahraini citizens are Muslim. The majority of Bahraini Muslims are <a href="/wiki/Shia_Islam" title="Shia Islam">Shiites</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-BBCShia_280-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BBCShia-280">[280]</a></sup> It is one of three countries in the <a href="/wiki/Middle_East" title="Middle East">Middle East</a> in which Shiites are the majority, the other two being <a href="/wiki/Iraq" title="Iraq">Iraq</a> and <a href="/wiki/Iran" title="Iran">Iran</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-BBCShia_280-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BBCShia-280">[280]</a></sup> Public surveys are rare in Bahrain, but a 2017 national survey found that 62 percent of Bahrainis were Shia and 38 percent were Sunni, which is consistent with most estimates.<sup id="cite_ref-Pollock_281-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Pollock-281">[281]</a></sup> Although the majority of the country's citizens are Shia, the royal family and most Bahrani elites are Sunni.<sup id="cite_ref-Pollock_281-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Pollock-281">[281]</a></sup> The country's two Muslim communities are united on some issues, but disagree sharply on others.<sup id="cite_ref-Pollock_281-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Pollock-281">[281]</a></sup> Shia have often complained of being politically repressed and economically marginalized in Bahrain; as a result, most of the protestors in the <a href="/wiki/Bahraini_uprising_of_2011" title="Bahraini uprising of 2011">Bahraini uprising of 2011</a> were Shia.<sup id="cite_ref-282" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-282">[282]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-283" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-283">[283]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-284" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-284">[284]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>The Muslim population is numbered 866,888 according to the 2010 census. </p>
      <p><a href="/wiki/Christianity_in_Bahrain" title="Christianity in Bahrain">Christians in Bahrain</a> make up about 14.5% of the population.<sup id="cite_ref-2010-census_12-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-2010-census-12">[12]</a></sup> There is a native <a href="/wiki/Christianity_in_Bahrain" title="Christianity in Bahrain">Christian community in Bahrain</a>. Non-Muslim Bahraini residents numbered 367,683 per the 2010 census, most of whom are Christians.<sup id="cite_ref-2010Census_285-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-2010Census-285">[285]</a></sup> Expatriate Christians make up the majority of Christians in Bahrain, while native Christian Bahrainis (who hold Bahraini citizenship) make up a smaller community. <a href="/wiki/Alees_Samaan" title="Alees Samaan">Alees Samaan</a>, a former Bahraini ambassador to the <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom" title="United Kingdom">United Kingdom</a> is a native Christian. Bahrain also has a native <a href="/wiki/History_of_the_Jews_in_Bahrain" title="History of the Jews in Bahrain">Jewish community</a> numbering thirty-seven Bahraini citizens.<sup id="cite_ref-Yaar_286-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Yaar-286">[286]</a></sup> Various sources cite Bahrain's native Jewish community as being from 36 to 50 people.<sup id="cite_ref-287" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-287">[287]</a></sup> According to Bahraini writer Nancy Khedouri, the Jewish community of Bahrain is one of the youngest in the world, having its origins in the migration of a few families to the island from then-Iraq and then-Iran in the late 1880s.<sup id="cite_ref-288" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-288">[288]</a></sup> </p> 
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:Gudaibiya_Mosque.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5f/Gudaibiya_Mosque.jpg/220px-Gudaibiya_Mosque.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="147" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5f/Gudaibiya_Mosque.jpg/330px-Gudaibiya_Mosque.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5f/Gudaibiya_Mosque.jpg/440px-Gudaibiya_Mosque.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1280" data-file-height="853"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:Gudaibiya_Mosque.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div><a href="/wiki/Gudaibiya" title="Gudaibiya">Gudaibiya</a> mosque, in Manama
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p>Due to an influx of <a href="/wiki/Migrant_workers_in_the_Gulf_region" class="mw-redirect" title="Migrant workers in the Gulf region">immigrants and guest workers</a> from Asian countries, such as India, the <a href="/wiki/Philippines" title="Philippines">Philippines</a> and <a href="/wiki/Sri_Lanka" title="Sri Lanka">Sri Lanka</a>, the overall percentage of Muslims in the country has declined in recent years.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (February 2020)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> According to the 2001 census, 81.2% of Bahrain's population was Muslim, 10% were Christian, and 9.8% practised Hinduism or other religions.<sup id="cite_ref-CIA_4-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CIA-4">[4]</a></sup> The 2010 census records that the Muslim proportion had fallen to 70.2% (the 2010 census did not differentiate between the non-Muslim religions).<sup id="cite_ref-2010-census_12-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-2010-census-12">[12]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Languages">Languages<span class="anchor" id="***REMOVED***"></span></span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Bahrain&amp;action=edit&amp;section=32" title="Edit section: Languages">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
      <p><a href="/wiki/Arabic_language" class="mw-redirect" title="Arabic language">Arabic</a> is the official language of Bahrain, though English is widely used.<sup id="cite_ref-BO_2-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BO-2">[2]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Bahrani_Arabic" title="Bahrani Arabic">Bahrani Arabic</a> is the most widely spoken dialect of the Arabic language, though it differs widely from standard Arabic, like all Arabic dialects. Arabic plays an important role in political life, as, according to <a href="https://en.wikisource.org/wiki/Constitution_of_the_Kingdom_of_Bahrain_(2002)#Article_57_.5BEligibility.5D" class="extiw" title="wikisource:Constitution of the Kingdom of Bahrain (2002)">article 57 (c)</a> of Bahrain's constitution, an MP must be fluent in Arabic to stand for parliament.<sup id="cite_ref-289" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-289">[289]</a></sup> In addition, <a href="/wiki/Balochi_language" title="Balochi language">Balochi</a> is the second largest and widely spoken language in Bahrain.The Baloch are fluent in Arabic and Balochi. Among the Bahraini and non-Bahraini population, many people speak <a href="/wiki/Persian_language" title="Persian language">Persian</a>, the official language of Iran, or <a href="/wiki/Urdu" title="Urdu">Urdu</a>, an official language in Pakistan and a regional language in India.<sup id="cite_ref-BO_2-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BO-2">[2]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Nepali_language" title="Nepali language">Nepali</a> is also widely spoken in the <a href="/wiki/Gurkha" title="Gurkha">Nepalese workers and Gurkha Soldiers</a> community. <a href="/wiki/Malayalam" title="Malayalam">Malayalam</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tamil_language" title="Tamil language">Tamil</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bangla_language" class="mw-redirect" title="Bangla language">Bangla</a> and <a href="/wiki/Hindi_language" class="mw-redirect" title="Hindi language">Hindi</a> are spoken among significant Indian communities.<sup id="cite_ref-BO_2-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BO-2">[2]</a></sup> All commercial institutions and road signs are <a href="/wiki/Bilingual_sign" title="Bilingual sign">bilingual</a>, displaying both English and Arabic.<sup id="cite_ref-290" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-290">[290]</a></sup> </p> 
      <div role="navigation" class="navbox" aria-labelledby="Languages_of_Bahrain" style="padding:3px">
